Of the woudes in the face. ca. xxxvij.
With this instrument ye shall lift up again the broken brain pan / all
alter as the one part of the brain pan is be sen up ward / and the other
part downward And ye press or vyse wherewith the brain pan shall be boren
must be very sharpe.



The foresaid instrument is good to work on the side of the heed / or behind
on the back of the heed.

This instrument is for in work upon the heed / when the brain pan is beten
in / for to lift it up again.



THe wounds of the face shall ye set to gydder without sowyn ge / after this
manner. Je shall take masticke / and dragons blood / and make it in powd / and
meddle them with the white of an egg till it be as thycke as honey / and therein
deep. ii. clothes the length of every side of ye wound / and lay on every
side of the woude a clowte and let it dry / the sow the clothes together / and
lay upon the seem this after following powder.

(...) powder.
Take dragons blood / ye lyme of egg shells / and white frankesence / lay
over it tow depte in the white of an egg meddled with the same powder / tha^e
heal it like another woude with plasters / salve / powder / or with ^awme. If
it be in a flesshely place and is not possible to beholpen after this manner
aforesaid / than ye must sow it with a round nedell and a small thread / if
the wound be dry / it must be sowed with a square nedell. And if the wound be
shot with an arow / and ye arow heed / or pellet of a go^e be in the body / do
as the foresaid chan^re shewith of the drawing out of ye pellettes. If it may
not be / so put in ye woude oil of roses / and lay on the wound the yolke of
eggs meddled with oil of roses / and use it till the nature prepare the way
of his coming out. And it hath^en often (...) that a parson hath borne pryuely
a pellet in his body a lunge season / and yet by ye course of nature hath come
out by himself And over all take heed that the wound in face be not frounced.

Of the woudes in the eyes. Ca: xxxviij.
THe wounds that happens in the eyes or the ^acou^ is great peril / because
of swellynge in the sight / and also it is ^yghe the brain / and also I have
often times scene of so small a wound coming hath stopped the sight / but not
all only the wound of the even / but also the wound about the even often
times it causes the fight to fail / if any moystnes be touched than follows
after the dystructyon of the even / yet have I known the contrary / For I have
scene child that on an evening by myse fortune of his fadet was stricken with a
sherpe aglet of a point in his eye / which aglet stack in the eye styll /
and afterward was taken out / and within xiiij. days after the white of ye
apple of the eye cone out / a spoon full / than was I set to the child / and
(...) put always in the even this water hereafter following / Fenyll water /
Rose water Poley water / Eusrasie water / Few water / of each half an ounce /
and of ye white of eggs. v. dragmas / and meddled them together / and dressed
the child there with all and recovered the sight again / In like wise a child
of. viii. years of age was hurt with a shaft in ye sterre of his eye / that
therein was fene a great web / thrughe yt which stroke he was blind / and I
died help the child with the same water that he had his sight. In like wise
speaks Galyenus of a child that he saw hurt with a sharpe iron in his eye
in so much ye watery moystries came out of the apple of ye eye it was healed
also / which cure is seldom scene. Jesu hath a another manner of cure / to came
the matter to come to the eye and if there come no blood out of the even / then
put Tuthiam a little Camfere unto the foresaid water. And if there have any
blood gone out / then may it be cured with Sedengi which hath the virtue so to
do / lay upon the eye where it is swollen the white of an egg / but let it
not lye lunge thereon / for it will take away the sight. For I have scene a man
of. xxx. years of age smitten with a stone upon the side of ye nose thrughe
which stroke the eye was greatly swollen / there was set a simple harbour to
take that cure in hand / and so lunge he let a plaster lye thereon that ye man
lost his sight and was blind ever after. Benemirus praises sore in this
behalf the white of eggs ground in a mortar as in manner of a faiue / and in
this substancye so made is great virtue given by the might of god.

Also if any person be wounded thrughe the upper lyd or nether lyd of the eye
that it hang down / then shall ye with subtylte sow up the sore with a
crooked nedell and a sylke thread waxed that the eye may swell the less / lay a
plaster made with the white of an egg meddle therewith a little dragon blood
with frankincense and loke that the plaster touche not the eye / and ye shall
make it as hole as the other eye / or also to balsam and that it come not in
the eye: and drop in the eye always the foresaid water for the defending of
that. Also if there come any thing in the eye yt grieves him / as small stones
or cha^e / or by (...) / to drive away the payne ye shall (...) in ye eye
woman's milk or fair s^te water for that cleanses out that ye (...) therein /
and if that help not / turn ye eye lydde and take a sylke cloth and therewith
cleanse it on^ / or with a small roll made of cotton / and if it hold fast in
the eye / draw it out with a small roll of rosell drop therein woman's milk of
a wenches^ turn the eye lydde then take ye little white stone of a swalowe
and put it in the eye it will run about the eye / and cleanse it / and if it
be the fylynge of ^ron take up the lyd of the eye and hold before it a stone
called Magnete and that will draw it out. Also if there be any passon (...)
with an arowe or with any other wepen in ye eye wherein is gre^yll. Nevertheless
go^ forth a^ ma^t of ways with the faiue of oil of roses so lunge as none
inpos^ macyon nor swellynge comes thereto / after that mark howe deep the
arowe he^ is in. Then work after the chapytre of the drawing out of the
arowe. And when ye arowe is out fill the wound with oil of roses meddled with
the yolke of eggs some what warm / and cleanse the wound with rose h^ny /
barley meele / and meele of fen creke / and then make flesh in the wound
and heal it with a grene wounded plaster^ or with a moist plaster. There
stands in Antithodorie / that if the arowe heed abide therein / ye (...) work
after the chapitre of the heed / and if the wound have need to be made wide /
work after the chapytre of narowe wounds and make it with a year of a sponge
/ if the wound be great (...)ghe / work after the chapytre of drawing out of
a row heeds / or let it abide within till ye time it come out by the own
nature. For wylhelm{is} saith yt he hath scene apson bear in his face an arow
heed an hole year loge / which came out by his own nature / than the woude was
cured in such manner as is aforesaid of ye wound in ye face / Also if there
any parson be hurt in ye eye with a nedel / or with a thorne / work as I have
said before of ye defesinu to noynte about the ^yen / heal it with this colerion
following. Take Boli armeni. ii. ouces / Thutia / sanguis Draconis /
Gumiarabici / of each an ouce / and make all in powder / and meddle it with.
xxiiij. ouces of rose water / and put it all in a glass / and hang it in warm
water an hour lunge / then do thereto. iii^. ouces of garnat appell wine / and
let it stand a day lunge / and than clear it out / there of put at every time
a little in the eye at ye evening and morning / and lay thervpo this plaster
/ take succy / semperuiue / succy solatry of each. iii^. ouces boly armeni /
draganti myrte / gommi Arabici of each. iii^. dragmas and make thereof a powder
/ and if ye can not get ye say of hows leke / take therefore rose water take
for nyghshew ye wine of the garnate apple / this water heleth all ye woudes of
the even plaster wise laid over it with clothes / and it defends the even
from su{per}flue moystes and evil accedent or in postume. When the blood
comes in the even from ye woude after shesus saying take thereto the milk of a
yonge woman and the white of an egg the blood of a down taken out of the
wings / meddle all this together and drop it in to ye eye and lay thereupon a
plaster made of breed cromes wet in wine / but let it not lye lunge that is
very good / if yt go not a way / tha drop in ye eye water of Amios / salgeme /
so metere / ye eye with dressynge of order / dry ^sope / if it drive it not away
/ take fair water wherein hath stand ^eed arsenicum / Or take the waters of ye
herbs a foresaid. ii. ounces / Thutie preparati half a dragma / Coralli rubei
/ margaritaru uon parforata^ / of each yt third part of a dragma Camfere the
wayght of. x. barley corns / draganti gome arabici of each ye wayght of. viij.
barley corns ^eru^e lote / amidi of each the wayght of. v. barley corns /
Woman's milk / ye white of an egg / of each a dragma / meddle the together
occupy it at e^e / at morning till ye parsone be hole.

For the woude of the eeres Ca xxxviij b.
IF any is wooded by ye eere or in ye ete wherein is sear yt the brain be
hutte or ye baynes of ye hearing it is in great peryl dedly for if ye bryane be
perisshed it is deedly / And if ye synewes be perysshed ye lodges to the
heryuge so shall he be deffe. Therefore must he earnestly take heed of it / yt he
give knowlegyng to his friends. And if ye woude be great sore / sow it lay
thereupon ye powder of ye lyme of egg shells frankencens dragons blood and say
about ye woude a defensuiu thereupon tow depte in the white of an egg meddled
with a little drages blood / frankincense / in ye second ^ ^yrde day lay in ye
woude oil of roses meddled with the yolke of an egg / for slakynge of the payne
/ ye other day after / ye shall cleanse the woude with rose honey barley melt.
termenty no / sometime meddled with myr^a / and sarcocolle / after the wound is
needful to be cleansed / or heal it with a grene wounded plaster / or with
bawme / and rule him with blood ^ettynge / to chambre going / for ye less
coming of swelling or spostumacio* If the wound with shot take heed of the
arow howe depeit is gone in / for ye pacyent may come lightly to chaugyng in
^brakinge and shaking with a cramp / and it is a token of death / and with
that token is his even reed / therefore it is peryllous to take such a wound
in hand. If you will do it ^rough great desire or other causys / tha tell his
fryendes all the tokens and conditions / that ye will not take him in hand but
for a deedman / than make the wound wide / as I have showed you before / and de
fend the place with defensiuum / than pull out the arow heed with your tonggys
/ as is aforesaid in the. xvi. chaptre / than clese the woude like as is
aforesaid / and make flesh to grow / and heal it like another wound / ^cule
him like as is aforesaid / let him dryncke no wine / there is nothing that
hurtyth the brain and the veins of hearing so fore as the wine doth /
therefore it is very good that the person that is woudyd in the heed beware him
of wine drinking.

Of the wound of the nose. Ca. xxxix.
THe nose is sometime wounded with a sword or with a cuttynge wepen / and the
nose is sometime whole cut of / somty me abides hanging on the flesh of the
lyppys / sometime he is wooded the length of ye nose Item is the nose hole cut
of / so may he not be set on again / if it be not hole cut of / the take ye
lyppes of the wound and sow it / and lay in ye woude roude tents of row / or
woll / or of goose fedders / that the matter may the better come out / on ye
seem lay that powder which s^ondyth in the chaptre of the sowynge of common
woundys / thereupon lay ^ow depte in the white of anegge meddled with little of
the foresaid powder / when you will than bind it and heal it like an other
woude with the wounded plaster / or with bawme / and take heed that he let blood
at ye vain / or let him set venroses or boxes behind the elbow / or behind
on the neck / and for to do his easement / for cause that no heet come to ye
woude / if the nose be cut over thwart to the lypof the mouth that ye nose
hagyth / or is cut through the hole of the nose thrylles to the over lip of
the mouth / so set ye nose thrylles the one an the other even richt as it stood
before / and make in the nose. ii. tents of wax / or of pypes of goose pennis
that is best / there through be clensyd ye heed and the wound / and that he may
have his breath / and begin to sow at the vpperst of the wound on the myddell
of the nose / and after sow on the sides / that the stytches stood uneven a
thwart finger the one from the other / upon ye seem shall ye lay a powder.

beneth / on every side one / thenaft bind it so as I shall say / thereafter
cure it with a plaster / or with the bawme as I have learned / and defend the
wound from heet and of evil accydentes with this salve

A powder.
Take Colofonie / sanguis Dracois / lapis Ematicis / Consolida mayor / and

Of the wound of the mouth Ca. xl.
THe mouth is sometime hew that the cheek hangs of / sometime be ye tethes
hewn away / and it is sometime with a sword / and sometime with another wepen
cuttynge. and sometime it is stytched in a dagger / or with a sphere / or shoten
with an arowe. In such manner as it happens yt the mouth is wooded / ye shall
sow properly the wound. the first stytche of the sowynge set on the next
place of the mouth / and sow it back ward going upward and coming down ward /
or at the side after the wound is. (...) lay on the seem this powder
following.

Take lyme of egg shells / dragos blood and frankincense / as it stands in
ye chaptre of the sowynge. Thervpo lay toywe or cloth deped in the white of
eggs needled with a little powder therein / and tha bind it / and at the. iii.
day vnbynde it again and thereon lay a grene wounded plaster. and lay about
the wound a good defensyuum. And give him to drink a wound drink / as it
stands in Anthidotario. give him soft metys that him need not to chowe / let
him not speak much. And if he be hewn in the cheke yt ye Jawe bone there ye
teethes stoned in / yt the one hangs from the other / than lift that Jawe
bone again / set the tethes by one hardyll yt to gyder with a wyre of silver /
sow yt wound / And rule him in all manner as is aforesaid Also make a chyne of
wood / and there over sow a cloth / and upon the cloth pache it with iii. or ^
it is dry / ^ for me / than ^ do the chy^e of the ^ end a hand of i^.
fyngers ^ de it together (...) heed wind it abou^ the heed / and sow the ends
together / and if any be hewyd that the cheke hang from ye mouth / that shall
you sow strongly.

Of the wound of the neck ca. xli.
AS the synewes and bades in the neck is wounded the wound is so deep (...)
chynge the mary / it is deed lie. The likeness of nucha yt is the mary of ye
backbone / it is in like the sykenes of the wayne. And come the woude not to ye
mary / all is it curable / for all that it is in great peril / for the
woundynge of the synewes which his beginning takes on the side of ^cha.
Therefore is said / as the wound is deep to ye reuersyue synewes so shall he
always be force of speaking. Come the woude to ye synewes by the ecre / than
the pacyente shall ^ And the wound in the great veins is vetyeuyll / for
cause ye g^te rynnynge out of the blood hastily departs the spryte of lyf.
The woude of Merry there as the mete gothe through / and the throut there as the
wyynde gothe through^ is very peryllous / for they have ye (...) of lyf /
therefore it is evil to hells. If (...) you come a woude in the neck yt is al^
through ye skysie / yt shall you (...) ^ of the small woudys. But if ye woude
be great and through ^ so shall you sow it / and upon ye ^ lay this powder
into wing.

A powder.
Take Frankesence / Dragos blood / lyme of ^gges shells / lay it o^ soywe
depte in White of an egg to the aforesaid powder. and than (...) it / the
thereafter heal the woude to a goene wooded plaster or Jewes plaster or with
bawme / and d^nde ye place that there come no unpostume nor swellyn ge / and
this is for the woudes which come in the length. If the woude be thward and
the synewes and bounds is wounded so set the synewes together / and sow it deep
in the cuttynge of the woude / and thervpo lay the powder aforesaid of the
eggs shells / and than there vpolay the groude worms staped and meddled as it
is before said in the. xiiii. chaptre. If the great veins be wooded it is in
doubt if the blood may be stop or not / if the wound shall be held or not
/ if that {per}soon shall dye or not but stop the blood with sowynge of the
vain so it may best be done. than make thereupon a plaster of this epythimia /
that Galienus set in quinto Therapentis. and take Frakensence one part / Aloes
half a part and make it to gyder in powder / meddle it with the white of an
egg in ye thycknes of honey / therein deep white here of hatys / lay it upon
the wound of the vain. or take this after following reed powder.

A powder.
This powder is made after the lernyng of Rogerius of Colofonie. iij. ouces /
Conso^ nor / lapis Ematicis / Masticis / saguis Draconis / Olyha^ / of each
half an ouce. all this make in small powder / and ^pyt it as is aforesaid. And
if it with that will not be stopped the pull ye vain out dress it as I have
said in the. xv. chaptre, of the stoppynge of the blood. If ye woude is behind
through the bone that ye back mary be wooded / it is very ill. therefore take
not to cure such a wound. If ye be desired to such a workynge / sh^ tell the
friends that it is a deedly woude / Nevertheless do your best. And in the
beginning put therein warm oil of roses / say thereupon oil of roses medlyd
with the yolke of an egg / so it less the payne / and as it begin to matter /
so shall it be clensyd make flesh with this plaster / as wylhelmus lacirancus
wrythyth and Guido saith ye same

A plaster.
Take rose honey vi. ouces / barley meell one ouce / torpetyne ware / rosyll of
each ii. ouces frankincense / mastyk of ^ myrra / sercocolle / mumye / of each
half a dragma. o^tu masticis. iii. dragma / of this make a salve / stryke it
on a clothe / and say it on ye ^ucha or back mary / it shall be better ^ ye
master or surgya think it un^ / but it is not ipossible to ye nature as she
may be helped with the strength of ye good medysyne clensynge. If the woude be
lunge / the bone wooded / and any pieces be cut of / so need you it to take out
/ if it be possible / than sow ye woude like as I have showed before / and
cleanse it heel it like an other woude / if the botton of the back bone is cut
through / or ye back bone by the neck / so as he has lost his meuynge / so
it is in {per}ill to help him. If it be not lost / help him with the
strength and clensynge of the rose honey / torpentyne / fenigreke meel / and
thereof make a plaster / and lay it on the woude / and about the woude shall ye
stryke defesuru / heal it like an other woude / If the wound be through the
throat goll / it is great peril / because i^. veins is in it / the which
veins comes to the loungis / and to the heart. As they be woudyd that is in
great peril and dedly / hereof wrytheth wilhelnius de saliceto / that he saw a
great lord was shotten in one of ye aforsayd veins by the throwte goll / there
went out one drop of blood / in cotynent the lord dyed / and the place of the
wound was black / he rattylde so sore till he dyed / thereby understood
wilhelm{is} that the blood ran to ye louges / to ye heart stoppynge his brethe
/ wilhelm{is} mind was to leet him blood on both his armies / but the time
was to short for him / therefore it is good in such a woude the pacyent to be
let blood / Also he saw a knight that was hurt in ye neck with a lunge
knyffe that the throwte goll was woudyd / he fill down lay styll / and
wilhelmus thought he had be dede / because he felyd no wind come from him /
and he halpe him that he came to life / Also he saw a presoner in the town of
Tremode / which of dis{per}acyon cut his one throwte / that his meet came out
again through the wound and his brethe / than bound he again the parts of
the wound well fast together / and laid there upon this powder.

Take dragantum / sanguis draconis / gumy arabici / of each like much in
powder / with this defensyffe powder / and plumacyoles or small kussyus of
clouthes or of roywe / and with the byndynge thereto at lenkth / he was helyd in
a month. Is the woude in this {per}tys aforsayd / with a shot of an arowe or
stytche with a dagger / and the arowe in the wound / then take it out softly
as is aforsayd in the xvi. chaptre. If the arowe be taken out / bleded sore /
then stope it as I have said in the xiiii. chaptre. And bled it but little / or
is there no payne the heed th^ with (...) ^nto (...) (...) wound upon yt ye may
(...) con^ pai^ or not If there been payne (...) may ye (...) wound. But is
there pay^ (...) (...) the it is needful to fylle the hole woude with warm oil
of roses / and put therein (...) t^ll re^te deyed before in warm oil of rofes
meddled with the yolke of tyges / and (...) therewith (...) woude open unto the
time yt matter comeout. The clese it like another wound / and heal it with a
grene wooded plaster / or with bawme. Some time the wound is shoten through the
neck towched the throwte goll called hysophagus / and trachea / but the
synewes and veins be not towched / then need none other cure than the cure of
another wound.

Of the wound i the schulders or between the schulder Ca. xlij.
IN this places to be wooded it is very {per}yllous / for the synewes which
come frome the brain / therefore it is dedly after the woude is deep or not
deep. Mark al so if the wound is in the length or overthwart. And is the
wound wide then sow it / and let in the nedermost end a hool open where
through the matter may avoid out / yt the woude may be clese / and lay upon
the seem this powder folowinge.

A powder.
Take dragos blood / gum of arabie / and dragantum and make it in powder. If
the woude be not depethe heal it with ye grene wooded plaster / or with bawme
like another wound. If the wound be deep you be feared for the payne of the
synewes / thene lay first in the woude oil of roses meddled with yolke of
eggs. Thereafter lay on every day a clensynge plaster. as I have wry then in
the a for said last chaptre / and is made o^rofe honey / barley meel / sometime
with turpetyne / myrra / sarcocole. about the woude stryke a defensyffe / made
of bolo armeno / oil of roses and with a little venegre. And as the wound is
well clensyd / then do flesh grow. and thereafter heal yt like another
wound. If ye wound be shot and that the pelleth or arowe heed is in it / then
make the wound greater / take out ye pelleth or arowe heed as I have said in
ye xvi. chaptre. when ye arow heed or pelleth is pulledout / then put in the
wound oil of roses meddled with the yolke of eggs / and all times stryke the
wound about with ye defensyffe as is aforesaid / and the next day cleanse the
wound. If in the wound growed corrupt flesh / then cleanse it with un
guentu apostolicu scdm Auicenna. And it is well cleseth / then heal it. And all
time hold the rule with lettynge of blood / to the chambre going / and with
mete and drink / and with myxed wine / after the dysposycyon and after the
coplexcyon of the wooded parsone.

Of the wound of the sholds and uppermost part of the arm above theelbow. ca.
xliij
THis woude is in sondry manners. sometime is the shoulder wounded above
ouerthward sometime in the length. sometime the arm above ye elbow is wounded
out th^ are sometime in ye length. sometime is ye woude small and through the
skyne / and some is a great wound through the skyne and flesh. sometime ye
synewes is wooded / sometime ye veins / sometime arteries / sometime the muscls /
sometime the bones / or the hole pipe therewith. And this happenyth / sometime
with a sword / sometime with a dagger / sometime with shot. Al this woudes must
you know to bind in sondry manners because it is a membre that is much
occupied / and needful to the body / and to al occupacyons of crafts.
Therefore remember well howe you shall bind such a wound like as it nedyth.
If any be wounded ouertwhart in ye arm / tha it is fere that (...) lunge
muscles or lacertes is wounded and the synewes that comes from the heart in
the arm / and it is to fear that the hand of the same arm shall have no more
power and that membre shall lose his meuynge / if the arm be hole or not. The
losing of his meuynge comes sometime by the woudynge of arteries veins there
as ye blood may not be stoppyd to the will of the surgean. If any be wounded
with an instrument that stiched and falls upon the synewes or boudes by the
muskly flesh there is to fere for coming of the cramp / and thereafter the
death. As the payne comes in the wooded place / than goth it forth through the
hole place upward in the bran / and so brings the synewes and boudes to
putre faccyon with ye evil matter And there falls often times great payne in
the woudes. iij. finger broad benethe the shoulder /. iij. finger broad above
benethe the elbow there as the lunge muscles is / and there the synewes be bare
/ of ye which aforesaid payne comes ye cramp / and through the cramp
comes the death. As the wound is of length in the uppermost arm that stodeth
on the shoulder / such a wound is not peryllous. If any be hewn through the
shoulder to the dysmebrynge of the arm and ye arm hangs / it is needful to
be well sowed / because the arm is great and heavy / than lay thereupon tow
deped in ye (...) of eggs / therein mydled with a little of the powder made of
gum of arabie / dragantum / dragons blood / thau bind and heal it like
another woude / and bind an harde kusshyn under the arm. Or make him a
croutche there with to lift up ye arm so hye as it is possible. Is the woude
over twhart that the synewes and other veins is wooded / than shall it be
sowed / and the payne be taken away / as I have saydin ye. xiiij. chaptre. If
ye said veins be sore blodynge / than work with the stoppynge of the blode /
and with sowynge of the synewes as I have learned in the. xv. chaptre. And ye
pacyent shall be defendyd from the payne yt the pacyent may rest the better /
and altyme stryke about the woude with the defensyuum of bolo armeno / olium
rosarn / and venegre / and lay on the seem that powder of frankesence / dragos
blood / lyme of egg shells / and white of an egg / with plumacyoles / like
as it is aforesaid of ye other wounds. And hath the wound but little bloded /
so let him blood on the veins / or with ventoses or boxces / after the pacyent
is strong / and defend the wound with ye desensyuu / and hold him to the
draugthgoynge / that to ye woude come not impostumacyo nor swellynge (...) I
have (...) before. If the synewes or muscles be he^ through / then sow it with
a wax dread oil of roses / that thereto come no payne / after bind and clese
it with mudificatyffe that bel^nthe to the synewes.

A mudificatyffe.
Take myrra / sarcocolle / turpentyn / honey of roses / but this mundificatyffe
shall not be laid in the wound unto the time yt you be sure that no
impostumacion come thereto Therefore take the rule of bloodietynge / (...) with all
things as is aforesaid. In thi^ place for shyftynge sowynge of the ^wes will
not slake lightly ye payne / whereby the uppermost mebre is the febler / ^che
synewes was used to serve him / than it is needful the stytchynge or poynture
to hold open / and fill the hole woude with oil of roses / and yolke of eggs /
of ye same lay on the wound. If in this place to the woude come a hot
impostume / then do as I have learned in the chaptre of the ruling. And if there
come blodynge with the todoynge of the oil and the yolke of the eggs for
takige away the payne / like wise if ye will stanch the blood with drynes
whereof comes ye payne / and if you lay therein ye oil and the yolke of eggs
that cause the bloodynge. For all that ye must stauche ye blood and take the
payne away / therefore it were good that the end of the vain with hot yro be
feared / as I have learn you in yt. xv. chaptre. and the Iron shall not towche
the lyppes of the woude. And tha lay shortly there on the oil of roses and ye
yolke of eggs for to take awayethe payne. If any parsone is wounded above the
elbow through the bone / the sow it / and keep the sowynge with this
afterfolowynge powder.

A powder.
(...) of egg shells / dragons blood why^e frankincense all made in powder /
and^owe depedin the white of an egg as another wound. Than shall you ye arm
spl^nt^ to the wound so less as yeinaye / that she may have ease that the
wound take no heet / that there come non impostume nor swellynge. To the bones
which were brokenor cutteth through / Idede make a holowe pipe of wood / in the
length of a spa^e and a half lunge. and cloven in the length in twain
lymynge the one side together with a clowte / and ye other side open like a
lantern / and therein I laid ye arm with byndynge about / and in the pipe I
made an hole against the woude / that the arm or leg may be in surte / and
I left the pipe thereon so lunge till the time I toke of the bound /
nevertheless I dressyd the woude every day as there to belogth. And I dede more
profit with the pipe than I dede with ye splynttes. Somtymeleft I ye pipe
cou^red within with askyne / therouer a lynnen clothe / for to occupy the
lesser boudes / for the multitude of baundes comes ready hootnes and
impostumacyon / there again I occupy all time that desensyffe / and on the
contrary side I let blood and keep him with good rule / as is aforesaid. If
the wound be shot / then pull it outlyke as I have learned you in ye chaptre of
pulling out of the arowe heed. there after cleanse the wound / defend it from
payne as it nedyth. If an impostumacyon comes to such a wound / it is great
yeopardy / or deedly / nevertheless help him in this manner. his meet shall be
pap of sterche sodden with barley water / or with lettuse water / or make him a
cawdel of almondes / give hi wine of garnat apples / or agros medlyd with
water. If he may not do his easement then make him a purgacyon. If he be syke
and feeble / or hath lunge lay be^ then give him yonge chyckens to eet dressyd
with the aforesaid erbes. Then cleanse the woude / and heal it with the grene
wooded plasteror Jewes plaster.

A plaster for to make ripe an impostumacyon.
Take the root of lunge malowes the ryne of the root / and make it clean /
and cut it in little pecys sodden staped to ye wayght of an ouce half an ouce.
fenigfeke meell / lynsede meell / ofeche. vi. ounces. dyll sede / camomyll
flowers / ofeche. ij. ounces. hogs greece so much as it nedid / and meddle it
together on the fere and make thereof a salve / and lay it warm upon ye
impostume / If he may not suffer the plaster / than take oil of roses / boly
armeni / venegre / a little opium thebaicum / semen Jusquiami / this small beten
/ and lay there upon till the payne be gone. then make it ripe / and then
cleanse it / and do flesh grow / and heal it.

Of the woude i the elbow, ca, xliiij
IN the wounds in the elbow about the elbow is great Jeopardy / for ye great
vayues synewes that be therein / for the blode is ill to staunce / not only the
stoppynge of blode / but also to defend ye wound frome the payne / or
swelling / or impostume / and a part of the cramp / for nyghe being of the
muscles / which be (...). (...) fyngers is above and b^the the ^bow / and also
for the bo^rs (...) the ^teyngys / and the vaynys that is within ye elbo (...)
they bring in much moystnes that is in the place closyd / for that can not be
so clesyd perfectly as it nedyth / and through such hoot^es of ye matter / the
feeling be lost Therefore take heed to stop ye blood of such a wound / and
defend the arm upward downward / for to coming of moystnes that makyth a
postomacyon / and swellynge. If than the woude be wide lunge / it shall be
sowyd as I before have said / and thee upon that powder of lyme of eggs shellys.
If the veins be woudyd so shall you bind it like as in the xiiii. chaptre is
said / and do it in likewise if there were payne / and o^er the byndynge shall be
laid a clothe iiij. do^b^e depte in warm wine / therein is soden the e^be of
mowseere / reed moder wort / or a^them^sia rubia / and a little cosalida mayor
/ and in this a lunge boude depte / th^rwith bind the arm and than lay ye
armr on a board / and that board must be so lunge as from the elbow to the
hand / and as he wylbe walking so shall the arm hage with the board in a
fouwel berynge on the neck / and bind the arm every time on height / that
the arm may be the quicker / and the pacyent may not much goonor stood /
because through such bangynge of the arm shulde come much blood thereof the
arm shulde swell. Therefore command him to abide styll / to this wound is
nothing better / tha to hold ye rule of ye woude in the jointure / as hyer
after ye shall ler (...). If in ye elbow be an arow or the arow heed / tha
follow ye. xvij. chaptre / if he will not goo out af your pleasur / tha put
thereto rose oil / make ye woude wide Inowgh whereby ye may have out ye arow
heed / tha fulfil the wo^de with ^ led with the yolke of eggs / or (...) the
whyt of eggs. and then cleanse the wound with a mundifica^nd / as I shall ^me
you here after in Antithodario. As ye be sure of the impostumac^on or swellynge
/ than do flesh to grow if it is of need / and than heal the wound with ye
grene ^ded plaster or Jewes plaster / or with ^awine.

Of the Woudes in the joints shulders elbows / hyppis / knees / and of the
glyttynge water. Ca. xlv.
When such a wound comes to you / if it be in the shulders / elbow / hyppys
hands / fyngers / knees / anclowes / and toes / and you fear for the
superflue moystnes or accydetes that may come there to. Than at the first ye
must mark if the paciet be full of fles^he and superflue moystnes / or full of
blode. Than ye must mark if the woude be great or small / deep or not or hew
/ or stytcheth with a dagger / or with a sword / or with shot / or ye stytchyng be
open or closyd. And ye must mark if the wound hath bleded much or little.
Ilso ye must mark if the wound is in a place there as the synewes / veins /
arteries / or muscles be. And also ye must mark if the wound is in ye length
/ or ouertwhart / or in a joint or membre that is much occupied / as the
elbow / or knees / to the which joint comes the most greets payne of the
great moystnes that there be sound of the nature. Also ye must mark or ye
pacyet is obedyent or not / and if ye must take heed of him or not / and out of
this points ye must take ye cure of this sykenes / the cure is to be done in
iiij. manners / The first through good rule of meet and drink / and of slepynge
and waking and of good rest / whereby the nature may be strength for to put
away all such sykenes. The second is / through taking away ye moystnes and
the blood / that must be done with purgacyos glitter lettynge of blood / or
with setting with boxces / and going to the draught / and ye contrary deele
rubbing or byndyng. The iij. is with dryuynge backward / restryngynge ye curse
of the blood / moystnes / to strength the mebre. The iiij. is with ye
consumynge of ye matter. And this is to be done with soft making of the
wound / of the helynge of the veins / or synewes or the mebre / through such
defedynge slaked the su{per}flue moystnes. Af any body be woudyd in a joint or
nyghe by a joint / that you fere that the glyttynge water shulde come out / If
than the pacyent be yo ge / and full of blood and full of flesh of moystnes
/ than ye must preserve him of much eethynge drinking / and let him eete no
fles^he nor eggs nor of no slymynge fysshe / nor meet made with spicery / let
him drink no wine / but he shall be content with pap of otes / or barley / or rise
/ or of almo milk made with brenne water / thereof made soppys / but little / he
may eete small birds / of yonge gootes / of all this a little / If he be syke
/ give him yonge chekes / but a little / his drink shall be barley water / or a
little Agros meddled with water / thereof he shall drink but little two times in a
day if he can do it / he shall wake on the day for that he may sleep the
better in the night / he shall the membre bynethe the wound lay hyer than the
wound / werby he may ye better rest / and if that he may not well sleep /
than stryke on the place under the eere / and on the betynge vain / and the
hands and on the nose thrylles with this salve.

A salve.
Take vnguentu populeu half an ouce / olei madragore one dragma / opei
thebaici halta dragma / Corticis madragore one scrupyll / croci / cassie ligne
/ of each half a scrupyll / cere. modicu aceri quod sufficit. fiat vnguentum
secondu artein. The other shall ye mark if he have course vnclene blood /
than meddle his drink with burrage water / with water of fumus terre / with
^nscute water / or side water growing in the flexce / If he has much blood
/ and ye woudyd vain has blooded a little / than let him blood on ye other
side that ye b^ de may be takyn from the other wo^ membre / and would the
pa^y^t ^o (...) let / than set him nevertheless (...) (...) boxys without
flemynge / if the (...) may not be set than in a bathe than ^ube bind on the
other side / as I shall say in the next chaptre Item ye shall also take heed
that he every day gothe to the draught onys a day at leest / and if he (...)
(...) of himself / so make it him with suppositories.

If the body be full of materies and of heet / than pure it with purgacy on
made by the pottykaryr / that the body may be puryfyed / and that the moystnes
of the wound be alteryd through another way.

A purgacyon.
And that shall be given him with sycore water / and with borage water / fenicle
water / the aforsayd reecpte is made all thus. Take Cassie fistule elect
nouiter extract. vi. ouces. Dyaprimis solute. iiij. ouces. Syrupi violati /
misces in forma liquida. or give him this purgacyon that is a little stroger for
coming to sooner to ye laxacyon.

A purgacyon.
Take Diacolycon. iij. ounces. elect de sue co rosarum. ii. ounces. and misce.
If he be syke or feeble / and so stopped / that you dar not give him ye
purgacyo / or that he will take not the purgacyon / tha make this glitter.

A Glitter.
Take ye erbe of Violet / herbam Mercuxialis / ^. malowe or pappyl / hye
Malowe / Beta romana / of each half an hadefull. water. vi. poude. this letbe
sod to gyder till ye third part be consumed. than take of it. xii. ounces.
and do thereto Honey of vyolettes half an ouce. Oil. iij. ouces. small rubbyd
salt. ii. ounces.

The third. defend the mebre for ye toronynge blood and moystnes coming to
the wound / and strength the membre that it may res^ynge and enforcyd
backewart ye moystnes. And this shall be done with re{per}cussyues and defensynes
with saluynge in the morning and in the evening / as often as ye dress the
wound. And in the beginning of your dressynge that vpperparte of ye wooded
membre roundaboute almoost iij. palm in the length / above and benethe the
woude. iiij. twhartfyngers broad from the woude. upon the wound and roundabout
shall be laid warm things as I shall say. And that defensyffe ye shall have
every time by you / for cause without that defensyuum may not good surgean be /
which all lernydmen of this conning occupied in all causys for all other
defensyues restrinynge the moystnes and other ill accidetes

An excellent Defensyffe
Take oil of Roses. iiij. ouces. Boli arm ni. ii. ounces. Terre sigillate /
venegre / of each an ounce. ^amfer a dragma. Nyghtschawe / howsleke of each an
handful / there out stamp and press the sap / an with all this same make a
salve / occupy it as is aforesaid / and as I have learned.

The fourth. Ye shall on the wound and about the wound lay things softynge
ye wound / and to brenge it to matter / and to take away the payne of the
veins. The veins to heal and to dress withtentes it is in two manners. the
one is better than ye other. The one is with warm and moyste making of the
wound. the other is with cold and dryenge and consumynge of the moystnes. but
all the doctors occupy ye first with the warming / al be it also that the
doctors whryten not of this sykenes / but alone of the discrasya / impostume /
cut tynge / and stytchynge in the synewes / veins / arteries / as I have
learned you. And this happened more in small wounds tha in great wounds / and
pryncypally in the wounds that be stytchyth / and for that ye small wounds
hath not bleded / and ye blode comes to the wound and may not out wherefore
the synewes / veins arteryes / comes to the more payne / not only because of
the blode / but also of the smalnes of the wound that the medesyne may not
come in it for the ^akynge of ye payne of the wooded veins. Therefore it were
very good to cut greater the small wounds that ye drynes may come in it. And it
is also my counsel / and I have found in it much prolyte.

One anoyntyng.
Take Oil of roses. one ounce. small rubbyd salt. half an ounce. and dry
ground worms in powder. a dragma and a half this well hot mydled to gyder /
and therein deep cotton / and lay that upon the wound so warm as he may
suffer it. and over the cotton lay a four dowble old lynne clowte depeth in
warm wine of the best that ye can get to the waygthe of. ii. pound a half
/ therein is sodden these erbes / mowscere with the yelowe flowers or Auricula
muris in latyu / herba Candela stondynge in herbario / Arthemisia / Beta romana
/ of each an handful / and deep therein the bound / byndynge therewith the
mebre / and keep ye rule which often I have learned you.

Will you help ye patient well and hastly / than he must suffer the wound
greater to be cut / and than do as I have aforesaid in ye xvi. chaptre. If he
will not suffer it / than work with the medecynes aforsayd till he be hole. If
ye have made the woude wyder or is it wide Inowgh of himself / tha put therein
warm oil of roses / therein is sod the lunge ground worms / and it
strensed again through a clothe. And there over ye shall lay cotton deped in
this afterfolowynge recepte and anoyntemente made thus.

A Recepte.
Take ground worms not dried and grafartysses / which is found under the
old moysty stones / of each an ounce / this stamp to gyder meddled with. ii.
ounces oil of roses made of lynseedoyle / that shall ye make as hot as he
may suffer it. There over lay plumacyoles or small cusshyns made of tow or
lynnen clothe and than bind it. If the wound be great / wide / and deep that
there any veins or synewes be wounded / tha shall you sow it as I have learned
you in the. xii. chaptre. and put in the woude oil of roses sodden with the
ground worms mydled with the yolke of eggs / and there over lay the recepte
aforsayde of the gra^artysses.. and do thereto an ounce turpentyne / and bind
it in all manners as I right now have learned you. This. iij. byndynges shall you
do. viii. or x. days lunge till ye be sure of the impostu macyon / swellynge /
and of other ill accidetes / that shall ye know by ye ronnynge out of the matter
/ by the dysposycyon of that wounded membre / and hold al times the (...)
aforsayd / of etyng / drinking / slepyn ^nd to do his easament by him self /
or purgacyon And than heal the wound like another wound shall be helyd.

The other manner
When that glydtynge water ronnyth out ye must dry and stop the moystnes.
and wasshe the wound with mannys water / Or put the ground worms in a glass
and stop it well / and wind that glass round about in thowgh / and set it in
an ove with other breed / / and with that water that comes of the ground
worms ye shall wasshe the wound / and thereto is better ye water that is
found between the shulders of an oxce. And as the woude is washed put therein
this powder herafter following.

A powder.
Take Bolus armeni / rede Myrre / white Frakecense / Mastick / Dragos blod /
Greeks pyche / of each like much / and this all together make in powder.

A plaster.
And then make this recepte / Take bolo armeni / an ouce. reed alom half an
ouce. and white of eggs as much as is Inowgh thycke as honey / and stryke it
like a plaster and lay it therouer. Or take this recepte here after following.

A salve.
Take bolus armenus an ouce and a half. reed alom an ouce. stuf meel of a mile
half an ouc. wheete meel a dragma. and white of eggs so much as is Inowgh /
and thy^ke as honey / and keep the rule as it is before said. It happens
sometime yt the woude is helyd / and the seuerynge or glydtyng water laid about
ye joints / and ye joint waxed great as a manys heed / and the skyn abides
hole as another skin / and these moystnes must be consumed in this manner.

A purgacyon.
First with the purgacyo of pillule hermodactuly / or with pyllule fetidis. The
second lay thereupon without that dries consumes / lay it plasterwyse over the
joint at evening and morning / it must well be bound with a lunge rollynge
bawd. shall be made in this manner.

A plaster.
Take Oil of olyue. xxiiij. ouces. Draga tu / Mastick / Gomi arabici / of
each. ij. ou. Boli armeni. iiij. ouces. roots of ambra / or consolide maioris
/ all together stamped pure and fine / do thereto white of eggs well betyn /
then occupy it as is before said. If ye will / do thereto Licium / Accricia
Alumen zuccarinum / ofeche half an ouce.

Of the woudes between the elbow and the handys. Ca. xlvi.
THis wound is not so great peril as is above the elbow / for all that the
lunge muscles is there. iij. twhart fyngers down ward ye elbow there ye
synewes stryngys be bare / that oftentimes makyth great payne / through the
cramp comet the death / this happynyth more in the woude above the elbow. Of
the woude benethe the elbow is small with out leasing of the substance / than
shall the wound be bound after that xij. chaptre. If the woude be length ye
arm or over twhart that it must be sowyd / than work and bind it after the.
xiiij. chaptre. In like wise do to the wound that is stytched. If the arm be
through cut / than shall you sow it like as another wound / shall be laid in
one pipe / or in splenttys / and in a touwell hagyd / as it is said in the.
xliiij. chaptre / and if the pacyent be not with a pipe bound / but with
splentes / than lay ye arm on a board / and between the board and the arm
with a kusshyn fylde with hey / in the winter fylde it with fedders / the hey
is for colynge / the fedders for warmnes / the pacyet shall hold in his hand
a roude ball / or a round stafe upon the board stondynge upright If the woude
be in such a membre / there as many veins come together / pryncypally ye pulse
or betynge vain / than take heed if the vain hath much blood or not / and
hath she not bled Inowghe / than do after the. xv. chaptre. Bleed the wound so
much that you can not so ^ it / than bind the arm above the wound as I you
have learned in the. xiii. chaptre. that blood shall you stanch after the
learning w^e. xv. chaptre. And than shall ye vnbynde the baunde from it / and
lay the arm so h^e that ye wound hangs not / and that the arm lay hyer
than the elbow.

A very good experimente to stanch blood.
If the wound will not leave his bledynge / then take heed of this learning.
For I Jeron was called to one that was wounded above the wrest of the hand /
and the seruaunt of a harbour hath so wen it. I asked the pacyet if he had
blede much or not he said nay / then I let the wound to be bounden to the
next day / and than ye woude bleded again. ye. iij. day, I vnboude ye
wound / than cam the blode sharply out / than bound I the wound hastly
again / but the blood ran out nevertheless / than I bound on the vain a
blood stone / as it is lernyd in the. xv. chaptre. The one part of the blood
stone dede I be thyn in powder and that meddle I with the white of the eggs and
tow therein I depte / and I boude it on the wound / and therewith was the vain
stauchyd that there no drop of blood ^am out / and than laid I the arm on a
board / and shortly cam that blood in the arm so plentefull that the arm of
himself lift up. Than laid I my hand on the arm to hold it stedly / forall
that it lift up under my hand. Than bawd I the other arm above the hand that
the hand was blew / and after that the blode cam in the arm that not was
hurt / and tha the wooded arm lay styll on the board / and when I saw that
the byndynge shulde hurt the hole h^ than I lose a little the bo^de and so
len I the handesom days bounden not to ^as^. And as I ye wooded arm vnbode
for to dress than I bound the other hand for the time the harder and for
cause the wound was staunched. viiij. days. I left the hole hand vnbonde /
and I should dress ye woude / and anon bledeth ye woude again / than bound
I again the hole arm very harde / therewith was ye woude stoppyth: Therefore
take heed when ye se such^ a wound than bind the contrary part as I have
lernyd you in ye. xv. chap.

Another experymentall lernyng for to stanch the blood.
Also was I called to a yonge man which was stytched through his leg above
his knee / to the which was called another master before / which thereto dede
a good staunchynge of blode / nevertheless the wound bleded styll / and I
desire not to come there / for cause of ye master / but I mark that the
pacyent became febler and fain tha I set the aforsayde stone / and in the
mean time dread I stamp small netyll And I cause the other master to vnbynde
the woude / anon cam ronnyng out of ye woudyd vain a finger thick blood. Tha
I laid on the vain in the wound ye blood stone and that powder of the same
blood stone mydled with the white of eggs / and ye powder thus mydled laid I
round about the bloodstone / and tha I bound there upon on the wound the
stamped small netylles and than I bound it with a rollynge boude that there cam
not out one dropell of blode. All be it so that a master laid the afor said
bloodestone on a woundid vain / nevertheless it will not stop nor stauche
ye blode / like wise as it hath happens me. And shor^ly I clean and shruped
the stone and th^ I laid it on the wound and tha he showed his power as thereto
belongthe. Therefore it is good to have ye stone in powde th^ to lay the upon ye
woude veins.

Of the wound of the hand and the fyngers. Ca. xlvij.
THese wounds be not so deedly as the woude in the elbow Nevertheless thereto
comes often times impostumacyon and swellynge / therefore take heed if it needed
to be sowed the wound / tha work after the. xiij. chaptre. and bind it like
another wound. If the wound is with shot / than take out the pellet like as
it showed before in the. xvi. chaptre. If the wound hath bled but little /
Than let him blood as is said in the. xv. chaptre. If there be great payne of
the wound / and the payne will not slake then make this after following
remedy.

A medicine.
Take boli armeni. ii. ounces. oil of roses iiij. ounces. opium. xl. barley
corns heavy. venegre / half an ounce. seem bisantie or by sant sede one dragma.
whyt popylyon sede or seem papaueris albt. a drag. nyghtshede or howleke sap.
iiij. ounces / meddled to gyder / and lay on the place so often times till the
payne be slaked / but not lunge for the coldemakynge of the membre. And heal
the wound like another wound. If after ye helynge of ye woude come there
small bollynge or knots that ye joint may not be vowen than shall ye lay^on
the knotten every day this plaster following.

A plaster.
Take powder of litargirii. iii^. ouc. oliu Olyue. xx. ouces. mustilaginis
seigreti / mustilaginis seminis lini / ofeche. xvi. ounces. mustilaginis
maluistici. viij. ofices. resyne. xxiiij. ouces. cere. iiij. ouces. olfum ca
momille a pound and half a poud. farine fenigreci / farine seminis link / of
each. iiij. ouces / masticis / thuris / of each. ii. ouces. hogs greece /
capons greece / goose greece / ducks greece / of each. iiij. ounces / all this
meddled to gyder make thereof a plaster / lay it on ye knottys / than shall it
be even Or make another salve to the same in this manner

A salve.
Take hogs greece / goose greece / kapons greece / ducks greece / ofeche. v^.
ouc. wax / il. ouces. litargirum. ii. ouces. oil olyus viij. ouces. farine
fenigreci / farine seminis lin^ / ofeche. ii. ouces. bdellij / oppoponati /
thuris / masticis / of each an ouce / of al this make a salve / With this salve
gothe away all the hardness of the joints / and the knots be even. If any
parsone is cut of ye hade / there shall a quycke hen be opened putting the
stump of ye arm therein / that shall stauche the blood. than shall you sow the
skyne cross wise over the stump / and thereupon straw the powder aspresayde.
as herafter followed.

A powder.
This powder is made of white frankesence / dragons blode / and meddle of this
powder a little in the whyt of an egg and deep therein tow and lay it on the
wound. If a parsone is the arm or the hand cut of / and there is any pecys of
the bone in the stump that is a little lose or not / that shall ye pretelly
take out / or is that bone not even and there apyre any sharpnes of bonys out of
the wound / than put the skyn back wart / and than cut of the uneven bone /
and sow the wound as is aforesaid / and than heal the wound with staunchynge
of the blood / and with the rulynge aforesaid. And occupy altymes the
defensyffe / which is need full to this wound. If this woude will not be
staunchyd of the bledynge / than lay thereupon this herafter following powder.

A powder.
Take Boli armeni / papyr brennyd / wine stone / of each like much / bind the
woude fast that the blood be staunchyd.

When the finger is cut through / than sow it clenely / and bind it like
another wound. And ye shall lay the finger in a pipe of elder tree / and than
heal it as I before have learned you.

Of the wound in the breast, Ca, xlviij.
OF the wounds in the breast howe they happened / speaks Gwido ye meruaylyd
him that he hath food whr yten so little of Galieno / Haly abbate / and Aniceu
na / of ye members that be holden / and much is whryten of the mebres which
holden / and in these happened the wounds in sondry manners / and therefore ye
must have also sodry cures / thereafter as they be. It is sometime done with a
sword / or with a dagger / or with a shot / or with a sphere through ronnyng.
Sotyme is wou did the vttermoost skin and the flesh. sometime in the holnes
of the breast. sometime through ye body. Sometime is wooded the members within the
body / like as the heart / / the longues / and dyafragma / that is the panicle
that departs the heart from the other members. And sometime is not towched ye
membrys within / tha it is but a simple woude. And this is needful to be
known of this particyon to the surgean / that he may the better do her cure.
And because that the old doctors in this cure accordyth not / therefore I
shall whryten some what for to know the manner of the wounds er ye take it in
charge.

The first is. If any be wounded in the breast with a sword or other like
wepen / it is lightly to known if the wound within be cut through or not / for
the greatness of the wound. But is it with a dagger or arow it is ill to know if
it goth through or no^ Therefore it is needful to have to^es fo^ to know the
same. And that token that the wound gothe through the breest / or in the
holnes of the breast is / as the wind comes out the wound / princypaly when
the nose and the mouth is stops / than shall you hage a little feder on a
thread a fore ye woude / is the woude through than shall meue the feder. The
token that the heart is wounded / is the blacknes of ye blode / and that the
members without be cold / the superfluyte of the sweyt / of swommynge / or
Sincopis minuta. The place of such a wound is under the lift pap / and
that is not good such a woude to take in hand therefore it is a cause for to
refuse it / for it is a deedly cure. The tokens that the longue is wounded that
ye blood coming there out is rede and shomynge / and the pacyente is hoot and
kowckyth / and is pale an ye place of the wound is on the right / And as
Galienus said / when much bloo^yth through the mouth without payne / that is a
token that the veins be hurt / The token ye the diafragma is woudyd / that he
cannot hale his wind to him / strongly cowghed with blood slymy spattyll /
that the pacyent hath great payne in his fyde / the place of this wound is by
the small rybbys / if ^herto comythyll accedete / ^han it is dedly / and if the
blood rya in ye belly / than it will corrupt for need and rot / and the
token of this is heuynes of the side / and of ye small rybbys / stynkynge
spatyll with much cowghe / the pacyent has therewith the axcis / and Jamer
said also / the wind that comes out of ye woude / and the mouth styncketh so
sore yt it may not be suffryd / yt clowte stynckyth of the blood that comes
from ye woude / and the woude that gothe through ye back / is in more peril
than ye other / for the veins / synowes / throat / and stryngis of the heart /
and that is fastenyth to the back / and the wound of the mary is also sore
peryllous / and the wound of the back bone that is not deep / that is not
peryllous / and the wound that towched not the back bone / is not so
peryllons. If the wound be small without leasing of the substance / and that
comes not to noneyll accidence / yt shall be dressed with tow depped in the white
of an egg / therein mydle a letyll drogos blood with frankesence / lyme of
egg shell les / and on the. iij. day shall the wound be dressed again / and
than heal it with a gre^ woundyd plaster / or with the Jewys plaster / or with
bawme / but will you occupy the bawme / so must you occupy it at ye
beginning to the ending without dressing of the white of an egg doing
thereto / and also defend the wound from hete and swellynge with that
de^ensi^um.

A Defensyffe.
Take role armeno / oil of roses / and a letyll venegre. If the wound be
great yt it must be sowed / than sow it and bind it so as I have learned you
before / and ye shall heal it like another wound with the grene woundyd plaster
/ or with the Jewys plaster / or with the bawme. If the wound be so great and
deep stytcheth or shotten / that it gothe in the hole of the body / of such a
wound said Rogerius / Brunus / Wylhelmus de Saleceto / Lancfrancus / that it
shall not be bouden / for cause ye blood shol de not abide within / such a
woude shall be kept up with tents / depeth with oil of roles / and with
salve / and stryke about with defenstuum / and when ye be sure of ye ill
accidence / than shall ye cleanse the wound with this.

A clensynge.
Take Frankesence / Myrra / Farina lupinorum / of each an ounce / straynyd rose
honey vi. ounces. camomyll flowers. ij. ounces wine one poude / and a half /
all this together mydled / and this in the wound s^owttyd / for to cleanse the
wound / and take heed that ye spowtte no more in the woude than he may suff^e
/ and than roll the pacyont on a table roundabout / and turn him upon the
wound / that the clensynge may comeout again / if he may that suffer no more
/ so do in ye woude again ye tetes / clese it with this plaster. Take rose honey
/ frakencense / myrra / sarcocolle / or take an other plaster.

A plaster.
Take strained rose honey turpentyne vi. ouces. barley meell / as much as it
needed / tha shall ye woude be clean with this plaster / with this plaster / with
the aforsayd spowtynge / that takyth away ye ill matter / and this is the
counsel of ye aforsayd masters / because if ye matter should abide in ye
woude / it would cometo ye heart / and to al other gentle mebrys within ye body
/ it would be the death of the pacyent. Theodoricus Henricus said that the
wound should be closyd. and if it is of need yt the pacyent is ^eble / tha^
shall yegyue him strengthyng confortynge to the nature / and if the wound be
not hastily closen / ye natural het ^holde through the wound come out / and
the cold air would go in / which air destroys the wound / but Guido
makyth concorde between the aforsayd masters / and said that the substance of
the things that comes in the wound of the breast / as well it is in the first
part / as in the other part And to all the woudes of ye breast belongth ^woo
curis / one win / another without / this woudes is to be known by ye for said
^okes. As you know by the aforsayd tokens / that there is no membre within
wooded / or that no matter comes therein / the wound shall ye heal without
tents / and with plasters salve incarnati^u / with plu macyoles deppeth in wine
/ with byndynge that seldom be changed / if ye be called to a woudyd {per}son /
that ye ^ere ye woude has bleded inward / that shall ye know by ye ratlynge
of the pacyet / hath he bleded a little / that ye blood rynnynge is done / than
command you ye pacyent to stupe on ye woude / that he may meue him that the
blode may come out / and as that is done / so let ye wound be tentyd with
medsynes or drynes that drawith outward / as turpentyne / or honey that is
better that drawyth outward / and dries / and clensyth. And bind him with
plumacyoles of tow / but not to styffe / in this same day let him blood / In
ye. iij. day glitter the wound with the soden wine / as I shall say here after /
than turn the pacyent on the woude / and there in do reasonable tentys / and
that it let not ye out coming of ye engeled blood or matter / and the tentys
shall be bound with a loge thread / because that the tent ^all not in the
ho^nes of the breast / for the pacyent should suffer much harm. If there be the
rynnynge of the blood / than ye shall not stop it / but if it rynnyd to much
/ than it is needful to stop it / yt the pacyent leese not his might. As that
blood is stopped / and the other side is let blood on the vain / and the wound
is glystert with wine / than ha^e you with the tents / and plastets that the
blood may melt / and consume / and ta^ge away the payne / as is these hera^.

A salve.
Take Farina ordei. xij. ounces. Farin^ ^enigreci. viij. ounces. Florum ^an.
oun^ Ab^inthei / Calamenti / and rubb^ d^anne / of each. iiij. ounces / This is
the best experiment drawing out the blood of the groude of the body. As any
^lede inwa^de through the wound / than take of the under crust of a lofe / as
hoot as it comith out of an oven / and lay the crom of the breed upon the
wound / as hoot as he may ^uffer it. And ye pacyent shall be laid upon ye
side on ye woude / this do as often as he may suffer it / till there in is no
more blood / and tha shall be give him medsyns to ^ele him

A medsyne.
Take bolus armenus / terra sigillata / with water therein is soden roses /
sumach / ballaustie / granorum myrtillorum / masticis / sytrinorum / or ye
shall give him medsyns consolidatyues / as terra sigillata / bolus arminus / of
each ii. ouces. Mumie iij. ounces. Sumach Sytrinoru / Sorboru / ^osolida
ma^oris / et medie of each an handful / sethe this in rain water / or in
water therein iron is quensyd in. Gothe the woude through to the inward members
/ and they is woundyd / than may ye yuge the wound dedly / nevertheless / doth
the best / for the nature is sometime stronger than ye wene. Therefore shall ye
take heed where ye wound goothe in / or touchyd / And is the woude in the heart
/ than receyuyth the woude of ye heart no helynge / because there is some veins
by the heart / as they is woudyd / tha comes the haestely death / and that
blood destroyed ye natural hette / Or is ye woude in ye logues / it may not
be well heelyd / but with great suttylte of an experient surgyan / and the wound
comes comenly to corrupcyon and rottynge / for the matter may not be clensyd /
than with cowhynge / for the cowhynge makes the woude wyder / also it is need
that the wounded members be in rest / for the longues is altymes meurnge / the
longue hath also a sharp suttyll blood / that not alone destroys the ill
flesh / but corodyth and consumyth the good flesh / (As Galienns said) The
medsyns that should heal the longues that can not come to the longues / than
through the lunge way of the maw and the liver / and there leesyth the helynge
his might / therefore ye longues may not well be helyd / therefore the pacyent
gettyth Empeti^i / that is that he matter spatyd / thereafter is he Ptisic{is}
and dyed.

As Diafragma is woundyd / he may not be well curyd / because it is altyme
meuynge / it is in the service of the heart / geuyng wind / therefore is her
workynge needly to the breeth / than is that also hurt / As ye parties within
be woundyd / there the senewes / muscles / flesh tokyth her beginning / of ye
which woudige takyth ye heart lightly great harm If ye woude goth to ye inward
mebres / and is ye aforsayde tokes in the longues not great / and is the woude
outwart small / than must ye make ye woude a little greater / that ye may do
therein the medsyns / drynes / and syccatyffes made of this alterfolowynge
powder.

A syccatyffe powder.
Take Dragantum / Mastike / Olybanu / Gumi arabici / Fenigreci / of each like
much. And his meet shall be of Bran water midled with penidium. And command
the pacyent to be styll and to rest without meuynge. If the wound is in
Dyafragma and the accydentes little / than ordre him such meet drink that
drives away ye cowhe. Also put in the woude a small tent depped in oil of
roses and meddled with ye yolk of eggs / like ye cure of other wouds If the
woude is inward there ye synewes hath his beginning / than it is good to give
ye pacyet to lyck or to suck cleuyng medsyns as Dyaspermata / that is most
used to cofortynge of ye breast / or Dyacodion / or Dyasymphicon / And that
yeshall lay about on ye wound / it shall be Embrationes with wine / or with
dryenge plasters and with oil. in the somer with oil of roses. And in the
wyntter with oleum Nardinu and plasters made of Calcantum / as it stood in
primo cathagenorum named ^yapalma. If the longues / or if the inward members is
not helyd in. iiij. days. so^ it not well to be helped. Nevertheless help him
with a good rule / and let the pacyente lay on the wound / and bind the woude
(...) the blode and the matter altymes may come out. And if the pacyet may not
suffer to lay all times on the wound / than may ye torn him on the other
side / but most part on the wound.

Item the comune medsyns or drynes to all innermoost members / shall be taken
the medsyns they most makyth softness swetnes. and that takyth away that is
most contrary to the wound / as herafter standyd. Take Erugo eris /
Pomphiligos / Lytargirum / Cerusa. Al is that these aforesaid medsyn makyth
fast and hole the members without. Nevertheless they opened the innermoost
members.



Therefore ye masters of this conning should take and give the pacyet some
medsyns couenyently to the cicatrices inwardly / these medsyns must he
swellowe through the throwte with out chowyug and there to ye must take things
yt is sower slymy / as Ypoquistidos Ealaustie / Gall / Psydie / Terra
sygillata / Sumach / Rose / these must be colyd with other stoppynge colyges / as
Maloru citonioru / or ^ube extremitatu / or Myrti / or with sharpe ^oyne there with
mydled Diadragantu. ^yll ye have a good clesyng / tha dohyma wassyng sode with
honey of ye best / for in all such medsyns is honey. If ye know by the tokens
aforsayd that in ye throughgoyng wound the matter is fall downward with in. tha
tarry not lunge to pull out ye matter. Wylhelm. cousayled it / and said also
that the wound shall be made greater / that the matter and the blood may well
come out of ye woude tentynge with a tent deppeth in oil of roses / and the
tent shall be broad without the woude / because it shulde not fall in / and it
shall be boude with a thread / that if it were fall in / that it may be pull out
again. And Rogerius comaundeth therefore yt the pacyent shall be tournyd on his
bake / that the matter of the wound may come out. Jamerus comaundyd that a
glystere shall be put i ye breast with a reasonable quantite of wine / And turn
th^ cyet with his ba^on a table rounda^ out / and than let ye wine com out
again. And if it come again as clear as it was spowteth therein and comes
there out not so much as there was spowteth in / than let of your (...)
owtynge and heal the wound. But Albucasis comaundith that you shall hold op^
the woude layng therein old tow / that the matter and other sup^ue moystnes
gothe out. command that the pacyent may belayd on the woude / for the matter
which there is gadereth may run out. And Albucasis saith / as there is past.
iij. or. iiij. days the time of the wound / and that the pacyente hath no
shaking of the heart / nor cramp nor streyghtnes in the breast / and that his
condition and disposycyon of him is good Than shall ye know that the wound is
in a good case / and yt the nature is strong / than less your tents / and
the cure. Yt ye mark that the pacyent may not suffer such medesynes or drynes
/ than less your tent and lay on the wound one mundificatiuu plaster made in
this manner.

A plaster.
Take strained Rose honey. xxiii^. ouces. Myrre / Frankesence / Sarcocolle / of
each an ouce / faryna Fenigreci / farina Ordei / of each a like much that it
be Inowghe to make as thycke as honey. and do thereto aly teil turpentyn that is
very good. After ye couseyile of Guydo give him to drink De torye / Castorie /
Nepida / Eariophyiata / Pipineile / Piloselle / the tops of Nemp Reedcowles /
Athasis or Tanacetu / Lycorys / of each. v. dragma. and sethe this with honey wine
/ give him hereof to drink a goblet full at every morning. And can ye not
perceive the thorough going of the wound with tetes depped in oil of roses /
nor with no other things / nor the pacyent may not suffer we sihynge nor out
pulling of the matter / and the pacyet hath heuynes and vpblowynge in the side
/ and other tokens / that is a knowledge that ye matter is gadered up Dyafragma.
Is than the pacyent strong and well content / than cousay led Wilhelmus / that
ye make a new wou de with a zesour in the neder part of the sy de of the
pacyet against the back bone between the. iii. and. v. rybbe. or between ye
iii. and. iiii. rybbes. for the Dyafragma is bowen with the rybbes and with the
back bone to the. iii. and. iiii. rybbe. and the bowynge might leave the out
coming of the matter / than would the Snrgean thyncke he hadnot cut deep
Enough / therefore it is the better to cut between the. iiii. and. v. rybbes.
And as ye cuttynge is done / thane lay therein a tent depped in warm oil of
roses unto the ground till the matter com me. and in the time of ye dressynge
/ dress ye wound shortly / that the air entre not in to the inwardly members
/ for that the out blowynge do not harm / And as the matter begin to apyre /
than shall you cleanse the innermoost place thorough the new won de with this
washing hereafter.

A clensynge.
Take wine wherein sodden is Camomylle Faryna lupinorum / Rose honey: al to gyder
mydlyd / taking so much as it is Inowgh and than strain it: and thereof spowte
one pond in the wound. Than shall you the patient roll roudabout and than
turn him again on the wound / that the wine and the clensynge may come out
again / than put the^ a tent depped in rose honey: Than lay thereupon that
mundificatiuum or the grene wounded plaster therein is much rosyll. and let the
old woude be closyd shortly. Auicenna saith. As ye thyncke be to much matter
in Pleuresy that not is clensyd in. xl. days. or les / that doth the parsone
fall in ptisym and to outdryeng of the naturally moystnes. Therefore it nedyd
to such a woude to occupy a cauterye with a hoot Iron / wherewith ye may
through bore the breast to the place there the matter abyded in / that the
matter may be dried that the matter may be driven out by little and by little /
wasshe it with honey water yt the matter may come out to the vtermoost membre /
and as the wound is pure clensyd / than go to the helynge If thereto comes an
impostume / than shall yon cut open a rybbe on another place of the side / as
I have learned you / and it shall be on the same side there the matter is / and
than let the mattter out / that shulde be not of need were the wound before
defendyd from the swellynge / as I have learned you. Take also heed as ye let
the matter goo out that ye make the pacyent not febler / therewith he should
less much of his might. And if the spryte of life is lesyd / than follows
often times the death. Therefore take good heed to him yt ye him the tent
intymes put in / depped in oil of roses midled with rose honey for better clesyng
of ye wooded place. And also cleanse the wound about with vnguetum apostolicum
/ which salve clensyd all wounds. Thereafter wasshe the wound with this after
following myxture.

Take wine therein es sod Myrre / and ye shells or ga^nat apples / and wasshe
there with every day the wound. And heal it with the grene wounded plaster:
and with this powder herafter following.

Take Masticke: Myrra: Frankincense: Sarcocolle: Dragons blood: of each like
much: made and driven in powder / and than with ye wine wasshe the wound and
straw therein that powder / and lay there over the plaster / and plumacioles.
bind it with a baunde deppeth in the wine: and heal it. Some manner of the
byndynge of the wounds in the breast makyth ye flesh: and sam holdyth alone
the medsyns. The first byndynge which makyth flesh must be broad and lunge:
and therewith begin the byndynge on the contrary side: and bind the. ii. ^es
crossewyse on the woude: and wind about with the band so often as it nedyth:
and sow the ends of the band on that first part far from the same woude
And Henricus said Thereto shall be knot two small cords / the one going on
the shoulder: and the other under the arm.

Rogerius for the medsyns to hold makes a broad bade with holys and with one
end and that other end cut: that ye shoulder may go through the hole of the
end of the band wind the breast roudabout / com again there you began. and with
that cutteth end bide him the shoulder. If it may be done without cuttynge / and
omwyndynge the breast / tha shall ye byndyng be to lighter / as he saith / For
to come no impostume vnd the arm so is the breast boude with a cuttynge band /
with the one end es boude roudabout ye breast coming under the other hole of
ye arm / and with the other end bind about over ye shoulder / comy again on ye
first side / is theraft bound vnd ye other hole of ye arm.

Of the wound in the maw molste / or pyt of the heart. Ca. xlix.
THe woude throughgoynge ye maw. is very peryst. As the maw wounded that
shall ye know by ye outcomynge of ye meet. and is ye place of ye woude before
/ than it is dedely. Is the woude of the maw not great / it is in a flesshly
place / than may it be helyd with one good sir gean. Is there any wound by the
maw or in ye pit of the heart through the skyne flesh / tha shall ye heal
it after ye. xii. chap. Is ye woude great and wide / or stryke with a sword /
than shall ye the wound sow / bind / and heal after the. xiii. chaptre.

Is the wound in the holl of the body with out hurtynge of ye maw / or onyother
mebee / than shall the wound be tent yd with oil of roses / and rose honey /
and ye yolke of eggs / till it begin togyue matter / and than cleanse it with
the plaster in ye chaptre of the breast / thereafter shall you do grown flesh
with vnguentum fuscum / or vngue tum aureum scdm Mosue / which salve I shall
set in Anthydorario / Orye shall c^cse the woude with this after following water.

A ciensynge water.
Take wine therein is sod mirre / and shell lyso of garnat apples / there in
straw it with the powder that stand in the asoresayde chaptre / and thereon lay
a grene wounded plaster / so lunge till it be hole. Is the wound in the maw
in his substance / and it gothe through than is the wound deedly. Is the woude
not great like as of a dag ge^ or another sharpe thing / than shall it be made
a little greater / tha sow it again which a thresquarnydyll / with awaxyd sylken
thread / pryncypally as ye wound is in the fles^hy part. If the wound be in
the vppermoost part there ^ynewes be / than is the sowynge for nought / and
upon the sowynge shall you lay this powder.

A powder.
Make it of white ^anckensence / mastic(...) (...). agantum / Gommi ara^ci / of
each a dragma / dragons blood / Mumie / of each half a dragma. and after shall
ye sow the wound without / and leave the thread hanging without the wound
there the maw is sowyd / as when that ye lose within the seem so may you it
pull out again / and on the seem without ye may say this powder here after
following.

A powder.
Take dragons blood ^ franckensence / lyme of egg shellys / drive it in
powder. than heal these wounds without and within like other wounds. and also
with wound drink / as I shall learn in Anthitodario. For all yt Lans^acus
saith that ye wound without shall not be cured. The wound within shall first
be cured / but that is not good / but the woude yt is with out may be changyd
through the cold of the air / and that should be the letting of helynge of
the wound within. And as the woude within is not helyd shortly / tha will it
never heal. Therefore must you apply hastily to the helynge of this wound.

And is the wound with shot / than pull out the arowe as I have learn you /
and fill the woude withoyle orroses / mydled with the yolke of eggs. Defend.
the wound with defenlyffe / The other day shall ye lay on one plaster
mundificatiuu / as lunge till the wound be well clensyd. And after shall ye
was^he the wound w wine in like wise as is in the aforesaid chaptre.

If the woude ve cut with a sword / and is great / than sow it / and on the
some lay this powder / of dragons blode / lyme of egg shells / white
franekensence.

A powder.
Item. ^ilhe^m^ made this powder of dragons blode / dragantum / gomme of araby
of each like much / for this helyd ye woude to gyderr. Thervpo lay tow
deppydin ye white of an egg mydled with a little of ye aforesaid powder. And
after cleanse ye woude with oil ofroses / and with rose honny. and wasshe it
with sod wine / like as it stoned in the Chaptre of the wound in the breast.
And yfther to come any ipostSpan^acio than straw therein this aforesaid powder
and lay thereon ye grene wooded plaster till the woude be well hole. Hold all
times ye rule of meet an drink / and lettynge blood / and doing well his
easement / as is aforesaid in the chap. of ye woudes in ye Joynte.

Of the wound in the belly. Ca. l.
Some time is the wound in the belly before / and some time be hynde / And the
wound ye es besore through the web or fleme there the boweles lay in. that
one is called Myrach and that other is a thynne panycle / and is called Syfac /
and lies within Myrach / and kepyth in him the guttys / on the hinder part is
the. v. spondeles of the run / the flesh there under. Also is there wounds
within / as in the many fold / in the guts / and in the maw / and in the
run / and in the moder (or matrix) and in the bladder / The wounds in the
belly is sometime that gothe not out / and sometime gothe there out that fleme
callydzirbus / and the guts / or other like. And the wounds happenyth
sometime with a sword / and sometime with a lance / and sometime with an arow /
and sometime with other instrumetys / that slyt chid o^ttyth. Some woundys be
great some small cut with a dagger / some twhart / some cleuyd in length. Some
little / some great / some wide / and some narowe. And this poynttys must ye
know^r ye begin to cure the woude. And herafter following be the tokens of
the wound / whereby ye shall know what membrys there be wonnded without or
within the body. The token of ye woude in ye belly going not through it is
known by the sight / and by ye pronfe / as that there comes not out. The token
of the woude of a strtche ye is going within / that is be known as yt proof
gooth deppe in ward / as zirbus / or ye guts / or any other goth^t. The
token that zirbus hath been lunge out / as it is chaugyd / that is as his
substance is black and stynkynge. That token that the guts is woundyd /
that is as the dyrte gothe out / And if the great guts / or small guts be
woundyd / is known through the place of ye woude. above the navel is the
small guts / under the navel is ye great guts. The token that ye maw is
woudyd I have said in ye fore wrytyn chaptre. The token (...) the liver is
woundyd / is to know in ye ^ coming of the blood / and the wound is in the
