right side The token yt the mylt is woundyd / that is the out coming of
stynkynge / and is in the left side. The token that the renes be woundyd / is
known by out coming of watery blood. Galienus said that the wound and
sowynge is more peryllous in the myddys of the belly / than in the sides. The
wound that gothe not through shall be helyd / with the grene woudyd plaster or
bawme / like an other wound / as is learned in the. xii. and. xiii. chaptre. In
like wise shall be cured ye through going woude within / as there no lymmys be
woudyd / than alone yt the woude gothe thrugh Myrach / and Sysa^ / this woude
is cured in the same manner afor said / it must have another sowynge. The
sowynge of the belly^ is in m^my manners / And Galienus commanded the sowynge all
thus. In the first threst the nedyll in the one lip of the wound / and to
wche with ye nedyl Syfac / and af^e^ that trest the nedyll through Myrach and
knet it on the wound. And in the second stytche / let ye nedyll godin
through the one lip / through that hole Myrach Syfac / and that shall be knet
without / and goo so forth till yt be all sown / Galienus said another sowynge
/ and Albucasis folo^ with the sowynge / and it is more commonly lighter. The
sowynge is in this manner / ye shall sow the border of ye lyppys together and to
every stytche a knot / as much as it nedyd. Albucasis set the manner with
the nedyl and sowynge / as is aforsayd / but let the nedyll stytche in it /
and wind the thryd about the nedyll / as is aforsayd in ye xiij. chaptre.
Lanfrancus Henricus said another manner / that ye shall ^iytche with a nedyll
the thryde without in the lypre or ye wound / and stytche it through Myrach /
Syfac / and so as ye stytche may be ordered right / thereafter sow the other
part of the lip outward again / cut of the endys of the thread / and let
it belong Inowgh / and after that sow in such manner / and let between ye
stytchis a space of atwhart finger / and as that is done / make one knot of
the. iiij. stytchys / and so goo for the in the sowynge of the whole wound /
and through the sowynge of Syfac / that hath no flesihe / be cured with the
help of Myrach / and with the woude outwardly / and the bands of ye breast may
we occupy to this woundys of the belly / there no lymmys be woudyd / nor comes
out: When the guts is woudyd over twhart / or is in pecis / tha it is dedly /
if it be length woundyd / it may be helped. If that the woude / of ye belly is
not great Inowgh / than shall ye make it greater / as I shall show you
herafter / than shall you take out pro{per}lie the guts / and sow it there
after as it is needful / with a skynners nedyll / Jamericus / Theodoricus /
Rogerius lay elder pypes in the guts / under the seem / yt ye seem rot not.
Wilhelmus some other / lay therein a part of a crier of a throat goll of a be
/ as the. iiij. masters saith / But Lanfrancus Guide they think it not be
prof* table / for that nature is inclined to outdrawynge strange things / and
thus yt help not therefore it was laid / and it is better that the guts be
sowyd / as a fore is said / and that it be clensyd of the vnclenes / and that
powder shall hastily be laid on ye seem / and is made in this manner*

A powder.
Take Mastick / Dragantum / Gommi arabice / of each anounce. Dragons blode /
Mumie / of each halfanounce. than shall ye the guts put inagayne / as I shall
learn you / and after that shall ye sow ye utter skin / and also that fleme /
there ye guts lies in / and in the uttermost seem / lay that powder in
delendynge of the same. And master Milhelmus de saliceto saw a knyghthur
tinge him self with a knife in his belly yt the gutttys run out / and the
guttys was wounded in the length. And to the same was fette Ottobanus of Papia
/ the which gave him ill conforte saying yt he must dye / for cause he would
put in again the guttys / and they ware so grreatly swollen that they might
not come magayn. Than cam the friends of the pacyent prayng him to come to the
wounded parsone. And as Wylhelmus saw the pacyent / than he co^ maunded to
brenge warm wine / for the time was to short for to seed water with Camomylle
/ and Anyse. And with the wy no he wasshyd the guttys there ye dyrte cam out /
than sowed he ye guttys to gyder again / and he laid on the seem the
aforesaid powder. And tha he would put in again ye guttys / and they was so
sore swolle yt they cowed natbe hadelyd / for the woude was to narowe / than he
cut the woude greater with a sharys / and put the gutttys therein again / and
he sowed the fleme / and also ye skin. and than he helyd the knight that he
lyfde many years after / hath chylderne by his wife. Comes the Liver out of ye
wound / is deppe woundyd in his substance than lysed she his workynge /
therefore it is dedly / for the wounds may not be helyd for the superfluyte of
the blood / why the is the foudament and matter of the life But is she not
wounded in any part that belongyth to her / than put in again the liver the
woude shall be curyd like another wound. Is the liver alytell wounded and not
sore / tha shall it behelyd of a good Surgean like another wound. And it must
becured hastly er it chaugyd / while it is fresshe. If the Mylte be wounded /
ye must well search in what {per}te of here substance she is wounded / And is
there apyce cut of / than fulfil the other place of ye hole mylt. but not as
ye port be cut over ^whart / as ye wound of ye mylte is hard and thereof
comes the water spring (or ydropisys) / and ever feblenes of ye maw. And
hangs the mylte out / than put it in again / And as zyrbus gothe out that is
ye fleme / and is that corrupted / tha bind or knot that there is corrupt /
as Galyenus saith / an cut it of after the knottynge unto the neder part of
the belly / and ye shall leave out of the belly the end of ye knottynge that
the thread of the sowyng of the guttys may be pluck out again as the woude is
clensyd. As ye seem is made in zyrbus / tha put in to the belly that same
zyrbus again and sow the wound in the belly shortly. let it not open till the
inner wound be hole also Jamerius Rogerius Lanfracus commaundyd. and the cause
is this. There is no thing that doth so much hurt the inner ^imes or members
as doth the towchynge of the vttermoost air / of the which air it chaungyth
/ and gettyth ill accydentes of pain / and hardness of the guts / thereafter
they may get the cramp / and after to dye In the wounds in the guttys
prasyth some masters the glitter of strong black coloured warm wine / and
prycypally as ye guts hath many hooles. And all his meets to the. viii.
days shall be thynne. for it comes not to rottyng / but to helynge to gyder.
And herto the. iiij. masters praysyd afterfolowinge. Lay wheet bran an how re
and than take it out again / and do there to this drynes.

A Powder.
Take flower of sterche / Dragantum (...) miarabici / Dragons blood:
Consolidama ior or Ambra: the here of an hare / and give him twyse or thryse at
every day. Is the pacyent feeble than give him the soppys of sap therein is
sod chykes / if there be said in Dragantum. Gommi arabici: it were very good /
and water therein is sodden Frakencense: Masticke / and this pray seth Wilhelmus
and Rogerius. If there be any parsone stytcheth or woundyd there his guts come
out / and they be not hastly puti^ again that they be vnclene / swelling and
chaungynge by the air / than shall ye take warm water therein is sodden
Camomylle flowers / Ameis. or take warm wine which hath no befulle ryppe / as
Lanfrancus said / therein shall ye wet a sponge and than warm the guttys
therewith / salve ye gut les with rose oil / put the guttys again in to the
belly. Theodoric^ cuttyth open a pyg / or another be in ye midst in tweyn
laid it as warm on the guttys so often yt the guts may lessyd of the
swelling / that it may come in again. If ye guts be fallen on ye ground yt it
be foul / tha lay it in warm gootys milk / cleanse it therein / than put it
again in to ye belly. Be the guttys swollen and the wound be small yt the
guttys will not in again / than commanded Galie. Milhelm^ / Lanfrac^ / more
other / yt the woude in the belly shall be cut so great that the guttys may be
put in again.

To this cuttynge is couenyent yt instrument called Samgatoria crokydlyke a
syckyl / before on the crokynge sharpe / but not sharp on the point /
Albucasys set it in a pycture as here standed.



The pacyent shall lay on his back hye against the woude / and the surgean
shall wisely cut ye woude yt the other guts com not out / than put in again
the guttys yt is out. And ye Surgean must have a good servant which shall take
heed of the guttys without with his hand softly / than take ye wound in the
midst cut the woude wyder / put therein again the guttys / than sow it as is
aforsayd. Thereafter defend ye seem the wound (as Galyen{is} said) with
Formacis that is called sanguinolentis / yt in other members ye woude makyd
fast / like as doth the powder yt holds the seem / the plumacioles with wine
/ and the plaster with other drynes that makyth flesh. Co myth there payne of
the cutting or sowyng as it happens oft times (as Auice. said in his. iij.
Canon of the cure ofydropisis) yt of ye cuttynge comes payne and pryckyng
Therefore it is good to put therein oil of dyll or camomyl / upon the place of the
cutting lay ye plaster made of lynseed / hye malowe seed / femgreke / of each
like much. And some other masters as Henricus for takyn ge away the payne
swellynge / they sethe wine with salt therein / and so much bran that it be
thycke / they puts in a bag and laid thereupon as hot as the pacyence may
suffer. Sometimes is done thereto the aforsayd oil / do it so often till the
payne be gone. The matter that is let in the belly and can not come through
the seem ye need it not to fere greatly / for the moysines can not be much /
they members hath not much blood as Wilhelmus saith / the nature resoluyth and
cosumyth the moystnes sendyth to the flanckys / and there it be enryd like
another ipostume in ye same place.

Of the wound in the bladder / and in the kydney. Ca. li.
WEre a woude in ye parts which hold / as ye skyn flesihe / it shall be
helydly ke a woude in ye xii.. xiii. chaptre. But ye woude in theumer lymmes yt
be holden / as in the kydneys and bladder / and ye moder or matrix of a woman.
This be mebres needful to every body / this hath no other cure than the wound
in the belly. That token yt the bladder is wounded yt is the outcomynge of the
vryne through ye place in pectine. That token yt the moder or matrix is woundyd
/ it shall be known through the place under ye nauyll through the outcomynge
of the blood / and cure the woude like another woude with sowynge princypally
when the woude is by ye neck of the matrix for there is ye more flesh / there
for the woude is the better to make fast.

The woude in the kydney may not be he lyd / for it is feded with the watery
blood that the helynge belettyd / and also it is altyme in meuynge / and it is
ye way where through the vryne comes. The woude in ye bladder can not be helyd
/ for it is a synewy part without flesh / is altyme meuynge / to receive the
vryne and to give out again / But the woude yt there is in the neck of the
bladder there it hath flesh and blood / that may be helyd with a truwe cure /
like as it be scene as the stone there be cut out. which stone is grown frome
lunge times in the body of a man.

When the woude is in the hole of ye bladder there as no flesh nor blood is
that the vryne comethout / that is deedly. For all ye sow the bladder / and
lay upon the seem this plaster solowynge.

A Salve.
Take Rose honey. xxiiij. ounces. Frakencese / Mastyck / Dragantum / of each.
ii. ouces* made in powder / and meddled with the rose honey. and lay about the
wound that defensyffe / heal it. And ye pacyet shall take heed for much
drinking till he be hole. As ye be called to such patients that is woudyd in
such a place / than say to their friends that such a wound is deedly. and
promise the pacyent always his health.

And first comaude the pacyent to be confessed as one that shall dye / ereuer
his power fail / and his wit change. and there after shall you work for his
health / for the nature doth often fail that the surgean thinks impossible
to help it / pryncypally as nature gets help with a good work.
Nevertheless ye shall not believe no good token that often happens to the
pacyente. For Ipocras sath / that ye shall not tryst in the pacyente that
mends hastly without cause / also ye shall notfere an evil token without
cause / for this dysceyueth often. And ye shall think always that the patient
shall dye / or else he is all healed.

This cure ye may take in hand in your land / or in your country there as
ye are known. But in strange lands take ye none such in hand / or other
woudes that is in peril.

Of woundesi^ the iarde or coddes Ca. lij.
THe wounds in this place is ferefull for her subtle composicyon / conyngly
workynge / nevertheless if ye yard of the man be wounded in length or euerth
wart / if the wound be not great it shall be cured with ye sowynge / powder /
lettynge blood an ye vain / and with good paysyon as another wound. And if
the wound be overthwart clean cut of / than is ye wound most in peril / by
the reasen of many synewes veins / therefore sometime ye pacyent dies or ye
blood be stopped

For easynge of the payne in such a woude. Take warm oil of roses and
anoynte therewith from the vpperparte of the yard under the nauyll / and about
Periconiam that is between the foundament his preuyte / brenne the wound
roundabout with a brennynge iron / with that ye blood may be stopped / and to
take away the crape which comes often of the payne. And thervpo you may lay
also ye stone aforesaid in ye. xv. chaptre. and also the stone made in powder
and there among meddle Dragons blood / white Frankincense / and Aloepaticum / of
each like much mydled with the white of eggs stricken on a clothe / or
plumacyole yt in ye midst hath a hole as great as a strawbery / and the
plumaciole laid thereon that the hole may come evenly to the pipe of the yard
that the vryne may com through / and over it shall you do your byndynge / and
behind roundabout ye shall lay the defensyuum And ye shall give the pacyent
every morning / noone / and evening the medycynable woude drink that I shall
set in Antidothario. The wounds in the coddes destroyed ye coddes / and takes
lightly away the generatyon all is ye parson not in no peril of death. And the
cure of this is like the cure of a common wound.

Of the wound between the hyppes / and the nether part of the back. Ca. liij
IN this woude is no great peril / but if it be very great / yt the skyn
flesh / and the synewes be hurt / yt the same synewes be schronke so shall ye
man halt. If ye wound be cut with a knife sword or oterlyke weep. If the
woude be small / it shall be boude after ye. xij. chap. If the woude be great yt
it must be sow / than sow bind it after the. xij. chaptre. shall be healed as
another woude with ye grene wounded plaster / or bawm / as it is aforsayd. If ye
woude be stryke with a dagger / ye shall tent it with a comely tete depte in oil
of roses warm / and with ye yolke of eggs and so put it therein as far as ye
may with the foresaydoyle / unto the time the woude begin to give matter / then
cleanse it and he le it as another woude. Je may ask me wherefore I have learned
to put so much rose oil in the woude / and that I have forbydden the rose
oil in the. xij. and. xiij. chapt. Thervpo answer I / that ye should be the
surer faster in your working / that there to the lesser com the ill accydentes
of ye hot impostumacyo. It is more profytable such evil accydet to defend /
tha ye ill accydet to draw away / for the nature of ye oil by ye warmnes
getynge of the fire / is swettynge / softenynge / opening / pryncypally in the
narow and shoten woudes / for a stycke or a woude come to matter than comes
seldom there to evil accydent. Therefore I comaude you to do thereto a little of
the yolke of an egg / for the woude to get a little cle singe / yt the matter
sooner should come. And al times is very good to make such an oil of roses of
vnrype grene oil olyue / for the ^ose oil such oil olyue have in them alytel
bytynge with styptica. Also ye ripe oil olyue is good to make ripe ye i'll accident
/ ye myddell ripe oil doth avoid ye matter / ye vnrype grene oil is good for
ye cosolidatiuu and for helynge together the wound.

If ye woude be made by ye shot of an arowe in this part of the body / than
draw it out take as I have said in ye. xvij. chapytre. Or supple the wound
withoyle of roses and there with shall it be tentyd for to come out / and lay
about the place a defensiuum than shall it be cleansed with this salve.

And after yt heal it with a grene wounded plaster / and the salve shall be
made of rose honey. xij. ounces / fenigreke meele / lyusede meele of each. iiij.
ounces / oil of camomell barley meele of each. ij. ounces / lay this thereon /
for it takes away the payne / and cleseth the place / his drink shall be made
with barley water sodden with parseley rotes / let him take heed of blood
lettynge / and to ye drawght going for it is needful to these wounds / and
command them to be quiet for there is no better thing for a wounded parsone /
and often comes of a little woude great harm because yt he takes no heed of
such a woude / therefore take heed in such a cause.

Of the wound in the thyghe. Ca. liiij.
THe wounds in the thyghe or in ye thyckenes of the leg above the knee it is
dredful for the muscles that be there / and they be often deedly. Guido saith /
there is but little dyfferece between these wounds and ye wounds in ye arms
but a lonely the wound of the thyghe is often deedly / and yt comes of evil
accedent / if the woude be small / she shall be boude after ye. xij. chapytre /
If ye wound be great yt it must be sowed / it shall be sowed after ye. xiij.
chapytre / upon ye seem shall be laid the powder of dragons blood / white
frankencence / ye lyme of egg shells / and leave one end of ye wound open yt
there thrugh^ the woundemay be cleansed. The first day lay in ye wound ye yolke
of an egg / with rose oil / for to cleanse it / and always do about the
wound defensiuu till it be well cleansed / than cause ye flesh to grow / and
heal it with ye grene wounded plaster / and at the beginning also ye may heal
it with halsom. If the wound be so deep that ye muscle be wounded / so we the
muscle with a waxentrede / and after the skyn And if the wound bleed to much
yeshal stop it as I have learned you in the. xv. chapytre / lay on ye seem
this powder / white franken feuce / ye lyme of egg shells / and dragons blood
/ and lay about the wound that defesiuum / and in the wound the yolke of an
egg meddled with oil of roses / yfye be sure there comes no impostumacion /
than lay thereon first mundificatinum / or the grene wounded plaster / if the
wound be deep narowe / and bledeth sore / stop the blood as I have learned
you / and let blood out (...) vain there against / and lay oner it tha^
fensiu^m. Je shall know also yt ye shal^ never take tents for holding ye
wounds open there as the woude is full of waynes or ^scly flesh / for in
such a wound comes great harm of the tents / therefore is put in the wound
oil of roses before the tents / for surenes that there shall no evil accedet
come there to / therefore shall ye woude be clesed and healed as is aforesaid /
And if ye wound have not bleed much / the shall the pacyent be let blood on
the vain / and rule him with etynge and drinking and to the drawght going /
as you may se in ye chaptre of the rulynge.

Of the wound in the knee. Ca. lv.
SOre dredfull and deedly is ye wound in the kne / for ye great synewes that
is therein / for the knees hath the most great bands Corda^ / and Neruo{rum} /
they be in the ^thermore part / there the moystnes hastily comes downward /
therefore all these woudes ben daugerous / for Au^c^nna saith that the wound on
the knee by the shynne is evil / because that to the wound comes evil
accydent / thereof s^e shall seldon be sure* and these wounds have little
difference of the wounds of ye arms / hands / of feet / but lonely this
wound in this place is dredfull / pryncypally if the be. iij. fyngers from the
knee / for the goodness of ye place / so come there any accedent that the good
surgean can not better it / As it is often scene / that the pacyent dies of a
little wound / and also he must earnestly take heed of the hinder part for ye
ij. synewes that comes from the heed thrughe the back and behind thrughe the
legs to the sooles of the feet. I have scene many evil accedentes coming of
such small wounds there is little set by or take, heed to / and ye mys^e
fortune is fallen to me and other ^o / therefore it is needful to take (...)
for this wound / and to follow after ye xlv. chapytre / yt speaks of all
such wounds of ye joints the plain matter / pryncypally of that glytynge
water / and take good heed to the learning of ye same chapytre / forget
nothing to do after ye rewle of ye same chapytre / pryncypally yt the pacyent
keep him styll / that the wooded lyth mebre be in rest as little as the wound
is / of the woude be small ye more charge is in it / therefore folowethe same
learning till ye wound come to his dysgestyon and gyuynge out of the matter /
till ye be sure of all accedente / nevertheless tryst it not / but go forth
follow the good cure / till that ye full wound be hole. If the wound be shot
with an arowe supple out ye arowe with warm oil as is before said / and the same
oil shall do as much good in the ^ounde as if ye tents were put in / an y^
shall strength ye said wound with defensinu / and ye shall let the pacyent
blood out of ye vain / and to do his easement / and as ye be sure yt there
come no ^stume thereto / so shall you cleanse the wound with vnguentu
apostolorum secund^m Auicenam thereafter shall you cure it with a grene wound
plaster / also take heed if there be any bones departed from other tha depart
it hole / tha sow the upper part of ye wound together / and leave the nether
part open that the wound may be clese thrughe every day / ye first day lay in
the wound the yolke of anegge meddled with oil of roses / ye next day ye
shall cleanse the wound / after that cause ye flesh to grow / and heal it
like another woude.

Of the wound in the shynne, Ca. lvi.
IN ye shynnes is many synewes yt comes down from the brain therefore in the
wound of ye shynne is great peril / not lonely for the synewes but for ye
muscles that is iij. fyngers byneth ye kne thrugh which many {per}sons hath
great ha^e by.

If there come but little blood out of the wound / let him blood on the vayue
on the contrary part. let him hold the rule in going to stole / as I have
saith in the. xlv. chaptre. he shall drink mydled wine.

If the wound bleed sore / floppe it as is said in the. xv. chaptre. then
cleanse ye woude / if it be need sow it and lay on ye seem the powder aforesaid
/ heal it as another wound / whereof many times es aforsayd.

Of wounds in the ankle. Ca. lvii.
IN the ankle is the woude peryllous / for it is set to gyder with small bones
/ Therefore as the ankle is wounded / it may not be set to gyder in his right
coposycyon as he was before. And also the ankle is fastenned with many synewes
/ and as they be wounded it is harde to help. If it be wooded with an arowe /
ye shall anoynte the place with oil of roses / draw out ye arowe and put in
the wound the aforsayd oil. And as ye be sure of the wound / then ye shall
cleanse it / and after ye shall heal it with a grene wooded plaster. the pacyent
shall hold the rule of lettynge blood / and of the stole going / as I have
said before: If ye pacyent be very syke or feeble / give him of a sodden chycken
/ and give them also meddled wine. And if ye woude be gretely cut unto the bone
/ cleanse it withinwarde / and then so we the woudeto gyder again. And if the
synewes be cut / sow them to gyder and afterward the skin / and lay on the
seem this powder of Dragons blood / Dragantum / Gomiarabici / of each like
much and in the mouth of the woude put oil of roses mydled with rose honey.
This do. iij. or. iiij. days. and lay about the wound a defensyuu. And
afterward cleanse the woude / and heal it with a grene wounded plaster like
another wound.

Of the woudes of the feet / or among the toes. Ca. lviii.
THe wound in this place must ye know if it be shot with an arowe / stycked /
or cutteth / if it be ouertwhart or a length / and if the arowe go through or
not. And if ye arow be in ye foot draw it out as I have learned you in the.
xvi. chapytre / if you fere there sold come payne thereto by the reason that
there be many veins in the foot / fill ye woude with warm oyl^ roses / and so
you may draw it out softly and lay in the mowth of ye wound oil of roses
meddled with rose honey / and about the wound this defensiuum / of oil of roses
/ boly armeny / with a little vynegre / and as you be sure of anguysshe cleanse
the wound and heal it with the grene wounded plaster or with Jeuys plaster /
If the cut wound bleed sore / than stop ye bledynge as I have learned you in
the chapytre of the wounds stoppynge / than sow it and bind it as I have
said in ye. xiij. chapytre / and lay upon ye wound ye powder of whyt
frankincense dragons blood / the lyme of egg shells / about the wound lay
the defensyu^ aforesaid / then cleanse ye woude / and heal it like another
woude. Also lay the foot hye keep it meetly warm so yt the moystnes come
nottherin. And keep the pacyent in rule of meet dryuke / of going to ye
draught as I have learned you. Also you shall know yt the synewes comes from the
brain. and yfthe foot be cut ye must be the more dylygent and dredefull to
take it in cure.

Dfall manner of fractures brekynge of bones in general and howe they shall be
ryghted and healed. Ca. lix.
OF the woudes yt hap neth in ye soft mebres I have said / as of the skyne /
flesh / veins synewes / unto ye bone So shall I write of ye harder del / or
parts of ye body. And of all yt is cut with a sword / yt the flesh the bone
be through cut / that is called a woude with the fracture or brekyng. if ye bone
be hurt with no cuttynge instrument / but with a clobbe / or staff / stone / or
falling / or cast / or by him self / or by another / it is called a fracture
or brekynge of ye bones. Sotyme ye by no breketh ouertwharte yt in the fracture
with the brekynge is no small pieces of the bone / yt comenly is called a fracture.
sometime ye bone is beten hurt / is uneven broke with splenters yt one piece or
many pieces depteth from other / yt is called frustralis / or ye bone broken with
pieces. Sometime it is not broke ouertwhart / nor with pieces / but it is rent in
length / then it is called Apertura or rent. The token of ye fracture or
brekynge / if it be in breed or in length / It is known well enough by the
sight by vnderstadynge / as haly abbas Lafra. saith / can ye not se ye fracture
/ ye shall know thrugh selynge with your hade on ye bone (as Rasis Auice. saith)
The payne of the bone as it is tow chid / the feblenes thereof is a token of the
fracture. And as there is in ye fracture splentes or pieces / then ye shall feel
by the pieces about ye fracture it will prycke in ye flesh the pacyent feleth
the pryckynge over the place of the fracture / most when you tow che it with
your hand. The fracture or rynyng that comes in the length of ye bone is
worser to be known than the fracture yt comes ouertwharte ye bone / but it is
known for ye membre is greater and thycker / that the patient with ye membre
may not labour as he hath done before times. That bone yt is ouertwhart broken
in. ii. pieces yt is more peryllous to bind and to hold / than is the bone
that is broken or rent in ye length. Therefore as such a fracture is healed it
is altymes vneue and crooked / pryncy pally as the fracture is in a bone yt
stands alone with none other standing there by / lie ke as the formest bone in
the arm / and in ye shynnes. The fracture by the joints it is the heuyest the
worst to bind / And yt can not well be bounden for there comes often times
great payne in the meuynge / hardneth the fracture with payne and mar ring of
the flesh / And if it have much moystnes it is evil / because he can not
be* help for the time ye fayntnes must be first amended. The fracture with a
wound has a sondry cure / ye must make holes in the band / through the
which it may be healed where as need is. When any frac ture is lunge unbound
it is dredfull and ye worse. And if the fracture ware harde / than is it the
worse to set to gyder / and there fore it is needful to have a great strong
byndyge / and the great strong byndynge is peryllous sor coming onyhete
cramp (as Auicenna saith)

To know in what time the bones may be healed / that is to know. The braynnan
may be healed in. xl. days. The bone of the nose is healed in. xiiii. days. The
rybbes in xx. days. and so forth it shall be said hereafter. But the age of a
man takes away such comely time of helynge. Jamericus Auicenna / and haly
abbas saith / what is the cause that the time is lunge or ye bones be healed to
gyder / It comes often times yt the bones be oft times dressed / and moystned
with warm water / and with often tie mes meuynge / and that there is to little
sly my blood / and through strayte byndynge it beletteth the membre to fed /
hyndreth him of his helynge. And it is said that the swellynge of the membre
would great pain after ye byndynge / slakes the swellynge after the time of the
setting again of the bones that is a good token.

Item Galienus and Auicenna saith to this fracture is. iiii. divers manners of
helynge and byndynge / The first the broken bone to be made even / The other
set together again ye bone that is made even. The iii. is the byndynge with the
hole / The. iiii. to amend ye ill accidete. And in ye first I will give you
that which is nedful to this work / first se that all thing be ready to the
setting in again. The other learning is to have a comely place / a comely
seruaunt. The. iii. is the white of an egg / so much as it is Enough / oil
of roses / and depte therein a cloth / after the gretnes of ye mebre. The. iiii.
three bands lunge and broad after the mebre. The. v. is tow that is soft and
well heccled / depte in acicrata wroge out again (As Ra. ^sayleth) The. vi.
spletes yt beeue smote / made of box or harde wood or of horne / or of iron /
or of leather after the mebre three fyngers broad over ye fracture / and more if
need be / as albucaiis saith / that it touche thought for hurts the joints in
the midst thycker then in ye ends / and thereof shall be laid as many as need
is / that may goo aborte ye mebre covered with clothes washed in the white of
an egg.

The. vii. if it be need ye shall have pypes with small cords to hynde so
much as needs after the length of the membre / ye spletes shall be boun de
with the cords with the pypes / and bind it reasonably / after draw thorough
the pypes small styckes so that ye pypes t^e not / that ye dyndynge lose not.
The. viii. that ye have suspenioriu or canabulu therein ye mebre right and
surely is be set The. ix. ye bed there as ye pacyent lies yt it be need / it
must have a hole wherthrugh he may do his draught and therefore he shall have a
corde hanging above the bed / where with he may turn self. The other learning
is of ye maky^ even with. ii. servants / where of the one shall hold draw
the mebre at one end. and the other servant shall hold, draw at the other
end upright that it break not / and if they may not draw it alike with their
hands / the broken mebre stretch out / ye shall take a gyrdell made therefore
/ or any other thing (As Ipocras said) but it were very good to have a vice
made therefore / that the bone might be preserved till the mebre be bonnde / and
the fashion of this same vice stands here in pycture



The. iii. learning that the holdynger of ye membre which is with byndynge and
ga derynge together / it shall be done softly with out any payne. Galienus saith
/ they that bind / shall chose the best manner that there to belongs that the
lesser payne may be / for not hynge mars so sooue the membre (...) the
payne doth coming of the strayte vyndynge / and unreasonable handelynde /
Therefore mark well (that Guydo saith) And in like wise I also have scene many
me bres that hath been evil gnyded with esti omena rottynge / Thereof Rasys
gives warning to the surgean for the byndynge that is lose bound it holds
not the bone / and the harde byndynge brings payne / and lets food to
come to the members / and the byndynge up must be as the pacyet may suffer
withease (as Rasys saith).

Ipocras comaundeth to take. iii. bands. And the first band going upward
from the fracture which lets the coming down of the matter. The other
band is from the same place going downward and that thresteth the matter
out / and these two bands kepeth to gyder the broken bone / and defends
that none impostuma cyon come thereto / but that is not ynowe / for ye third
band must be put thereto / whi che is bound for the keep the plumacyoles to
close it withal. Galienus comaundeth to take Acerotum / wherefore now is take
oil of roses. Also Rasys counsayleth that the fracture at the first
beginning shall not be boude to fast till the. vii. day. And on the. vii.
day. it shall be bound meetly strayte / and in the first and the last end
bind it not to fast for fere of impostumacyon. The. iiii. learning is that in
the be gynnyng shall be laid good even splentes or other things in their
stead / not for to strain ye membre / but lonely for to keep it unto the
seventh day end / for fear that ^o impostnmacyon come thereto / than shall
yo^ay thereon good strong splentes / that ye membre may be hold fast / and at
ye first time I take the good splentes / and bind them some what lose / that
no swellynge nor impostumacyon come thereto / And also do so to the last till it
be well strengthed / and be not hasty to take of the splentes (as Aup cenna
saith in his book of Sargery.

The. v. lernyng and doctrine is of the tie me of chaugynge or byndynge / or
dressing The chaungynge shall not be done before the. x. xv. or. xx. day /
pryncypally as ye byndynge is dressyd with the oil aforesaid / And if the
fracture be not well and right set / or any strong accydente comes therein
the sooner it must be dressed. And if ye dres se the byndynge with the powder
as I shall learn you / so leave the byndynge thereon ye. ix. or. x. day. and lunge
abiding in (...) a byndynge wichout chaungyng it is euy^ (as Rasys saith) But
if ye were in fear yt it is not well and right set / tha may ye change the
byndynge in. vii. days. and sometime sooner. and ye shall sometime change ye
byndyng. in. x. days. for cause than is yet there not come the porus. and is
than there any uneven than make it even yt it not pryc kyth nor stytchyd in the
fracture.

If payne / or impostumacyon / or other ill accydentes comes thereto / than may
ye change it at the. iii. day. But in other cau sys shall it not be changed
nor losyd up be fore the. vii. day. but if ye matter must be clensyd than ye
may the sooner change the byndynge and dressynge of the same.

The. vi. doctrine and learning is this ^itacyons of the porus / it grow that
the^ ^day / and when it is come ye shall strength him with meet and drink /
and Galienus saith the body f^de with meet where in is great moystnes / thereof
comes not only good / but also (...) my moystnes / thereof and therout comes
that couglutu (...) that is he ling to gyder (as A uicena saith like asrys
rap where / sodden heeds / feetes of beestes / and refrain wine. He shall
be ware him for all things that bren neth the blood / as strong wine /
garlyke / onyons / mustatde / and well smelling spices. And Rasys saith / in
the beginning shall he change his rulynge / in drinking no wine / and in
going to chambre / and in lettynge blood / and there with shall be lets the
impostumacyon. and as he is sure of the impostume than may he return again to
his old rule. In the first return again to the making even of the bones
with drawing out the membre straight / and ^y^e up the bone that is pressed
down / threste down ye other bone that stands on hight and do it without
payne / unto the time the bones be knyt together in his right place. The other
learning is to be done with a good byndynge as many master saith / and some bind
the fracture even with out splentes unto the fyfthe or syxte day / some lays
with plu macyoles of clowtes (as master Petrus) some of tow (as Theodoric^) and
they bind there upon the splentes / and these both doth h^me / for the
plumacyoles should be somany that there should be no fast byndynge and in a
slyppe might the membre be hurt And Euydo follows after ye said lernyng And
at the first do that ye payne may be put away and keeping also the membre. The
second in keeping that the porus or coglutinatiuum grows about the fracture.

The third / that in keeping the membre shall be stretched brought again in his
old workynge. Also there is. iiii. manners for to heal bind this fracture. The
first as the fracture is ouert wharte / and if there be no small pieces of bones
to take out / than shall the fracture and wounded membre be stretch dout and
hold by your servants and to set right the broke bones and do after the rule
as I shall learn you / if there be any small ^eces departed fro the bone ye
stycke in the flesh and is hanging on the bone set them in their first place
and bind them well together / make the small bones in the muscles payne and
cause them to follow the impostumacyon / than it is need to cut the flesh on
that place and take out the lose bones and than to make ye mebre hole as I
shall learn you / if the flesh be bruised with the fracture of the bone / then
shall the bone be bound with oil of roses and shall lay on the powder of
myrtylle beryes / and over the oil and powder yeshallay a cloth depte in the
white of eggs oil of roses bind it softly / If ye fracture be without payne
/ and without small pieces of bones is well streyghtened / so shall you bind it
with a lunge broad band after ye dretnes of ye membre without being lunge of
doyng And your seruaunt must hold the membre while ye clowtes be laid between
(as Galienus is wont to do* the clowtes may not be to cowrse for yt lets
the good byndyng and the same clowtes should be depte in the white of eggs
meddled in oil of roses / and begin to bind up ye fracture with the ban^e going
up and down / and let ye band go enough on the hole part / and upon the
fracture bind even without pain / and upon ye band shall be laid. ii.
dowble (...) depte and wrong out again / and shall cover the hole membre and
that the ends of ye splentes hurt not the membre / sow then the clothe about
and thereupon lay the splentes made of wood or of ledder then bind it with pypes
/ then shall the pacyent be laid in a still place where he may have rest / and
if it be needful ye shall let ye pacyent blood the other day / and give him a
laxatyue in the first days / and the byndynge shall not be chanuged in. x. or.
xv. days / without were any evil accydent coming thereto / or that the
fracture be not set well to gyder as is before said / and after the. iii. and.
iiii. day ye fracture shall be dressed / and bound as it was before. And if
the time be hot there come a swellynge / or impostumacyo or any other / ye
shall occupy the oil aforesaid / and if he be sure of the evil accedent than
shall ye make this powder / whereof I have often scene great profit come.

A powder.
Take bol^ armen^ / ambra or walderote / of each. iiii. ounces / small barlye
meal. vi. ounces / meddled all to gyder. And I was wont to do thereto walstone.
iii. ounces / they be brought out of Zwytselonde / or ex ^uincia Heluetiarum.



Nota I have (...) her de a surge an made his booste to heal in. viii. days a
parsoue that has broken his leg or arm. And this to prove a dogs leg
was broken / than meddled he this powder afor said with ye white of an egg /
and laid it on te dogs leg and splented it / and as ye. viii. days were
past / than toke he the splentes of / and the dogs leg was hole. Terfore
this is my counsel to take this store for this cure. that powder has such
nature / if there come any swellynge to the bone at the first time so gothe the
swellynge shortly away with the said powder. The other manner is that after
the. xii. or. xv. days as porus begynue to come / that is to know when the
payne is gone / and there come none impostumacyon / and ye membre is of good
colour / than shall ye take of the band / and wasshe the membre in warm water
lay thervpo a clothe stricken / and bind it with all the bands and splentes a
foresaid / and bind it some what faster. The fracture shall be salved and dressed
the. vii. or. ix. day. or more unto the time that the bone be well bound with
that porus that is well know by me uynge and by the vnswellynge. The. iii.
manner is that the membre shall be washed all three days with good wine wherein
is sodden roses / wormode or abscintium in (...) tyne / oken wood / and bound
with (...) dept in ye same wine and pressyd out (...) and bound with. iii. or.
iiii. splentes / (...) shall the membre be brought by little and little to his
old workynge / and at the last shall the membre be made soft with Dyal the a
/ or oxcicrocium. The fourth manner is when the accydente is coming / like if
there were payne or impostumacyon / and if the payne or impostumacyon be great /
that take away or it be boude with the lunge band that there to belongs. And if
it were well bound after his belonging / and there com an impostumacyo or an
unreasonable payne / there with is the surgean compelled ye mebre to open and to
vnbynde / and to amend the accydent / and to help the nature. And that membre
shall bestyll vnboundetyll ye time the accydent and payne be take away Therefore
must ye take away the payne (as (...) saith) with the saluyng of warm oil of
roses. Or (as Guido saith) with wolle depte in oil of roses / venegre / and
laid there over. And if there com scabs or itchynge / than lose ye byndynge /
and make ye bone moist with warm water wherein is sod a little salt that
membre there with embroced and drop with the water from above on the membre Or
salve the membre with un guentum album / or populeum as is before saith. When
all the accydentes is taken away tha turn again to the byndyng of the
membre. If there be a wound where in be small pieces of bones they must be take
out / and heal the woude as I have said in the chaptre of broken bones. And
after goo to the helynge as is before said. As the matter comes out / make an
hole in the band / where through the wound may be cleansed.

Of the brekyng of the nose, Ca, lx,
MAnytymes is the bone of ye nose broke / sometime beten flat / to which
brekynge as thereto betimes is take heed it may behol pen at his first proporcyo
but as it not be done betimes / it will be so every more styll. There fore it is
needful for the wound of ye nose shortly to be taken heed to / and to be set
in his stead. Therefore right it anon / for if it become harde / that doth the
pacyent great payne as it shall beryghted again. And ye must right the nose
after this manner.

Take your fore finger of your lift hand or your little finger and put it in
his nose / and with your right hand threst up ye fracture / and lift up with
your forefynger the nose and set the bone in again in his right place / when ye
have made it even / ye shall make a wtyeke of wax and meddle therein a little
powder of mastick / and dragos blode made in small powder / and this wyeke must
be made like a candle and put it in ye nose. Wylhelm^ placentin^ makes an
harde wyeke or tent of tow depte in oil ofroses / cast thereon this powder
made thus.

A Powder.
Take Bolus armenus. ii. ounces. Myrtil lorum. iiii. ounces. Dragons blood /
Dragantum / Gommy arabici / of each half an ounce. and take clowtes and
plumacioles depte in whyt of eggs / the powder strawed thereupon / and lay it
properly after ye form of ye nose / as I have learned you in the chaptre of
the wound in the face. I^ ye cannot put the finger in ye nose / then make a
tent of wood covered with old lynns clothe depte in oil of roses and put it
in to the nose / and therewith threst upward in to ye nose / and with your
other hand threst downward unto ye time ye bone be brought in to his right
place again / than dress it with the white of an egg / and with that powder
as is before said / and about ye place stryke that defensyffe of bolo armeno
and oil of coses / and a little vynagre mydled with ye say of uyghtshawd or of
solatru in latyne. and do this every day. And when ye shall dress this nose /
first let him blood on the hand / or with boxces on the shoulder or with
ga^ssynge / and with drawght going / with supposytoria or clysters / and yt
shall lighten the pacyent sore. And as you dress him the second time renew
ye tent with oil of roses and with the aforesaid powder / And upon the nose
lay the foresaid powder of dragantu mydled with the white of an egg and dress
the nose therwyth at the. iii. or. iiii. day. And if the fracture or brekynge
of ye nose is sore wooded / sow the skin together as I have said in the
chaptre of the wound of the nose. And lay the powder upon ye wound / and heal
it as in the chaptre a foresaid. dress the woude every day twyse / and rule
him with meet and drink / pryncypally he shall drink reed wine mydled with
water.

Of the brekynge fracture of the brayn pan I have said in the chatre of the

Of the brekynge of the Jawbone tethes. Ca, lxi,
AS the chekebone is broken / ye shall take your finger of ye lift hand and
set it under ye mowthe of the pacyent / set ye cheke bone even right to gyder
with your right hand you shall know when they stand even / that is as the syke
tethes stand even like ye other hole tethes. Then take a sylke thread. v. or.
vi. dobble well waxed / or take a copper wire yt has ^en ^eneled in the fire
/ and or it be cold through braid the tethes together in like wise as the
picture shows.



And after that take powder as hereafter follows meddled with ye white of an
egg / and stryke it upon a clothe like a plaster bind it as is aforesaid in
the chaptre of ye wound in the chekebone. Or lay plu^oles with dowble fold
lynnen clothe / and thervpo lay sklentes made of ledder. Than shall you bind
it with one band / be^nynge behind the neck and coming on ye cheke / and
again under the eeres behind ye heed / and so bind it about the foreheed and
if it be needful wind it often about in the same manner till ye band be made
fast And the powder is made thus.

A Powder.

^ce. and made of these a powder / and ^ly let them blood / or bore them

Of the brekyng of the neck and back bone. Ca. lxij.
PAulus (...) Haly ^bas saith / In the spondyles or back bone commeth^
fracture or brekynge / but of strong strokes are fore bruised: that is deedly /
therefore (...) following after ye evil tokenyng and accydent and hurtynge of
nucha or mary of ye backebone yt comes from ye brain down (...) through the
backebone / for the brain may not suffer. Therefore saith Paulus Haly abbas
Albucasys / what accydente or payne comes to nucha and to the synewes / that
brengeth paralisis of the hands as it is in the vppermoost part of ye
spodyles. But if the payne be in the nether spondyles so comes the paralisys
in ye foot (as Albucasys saith) As ye se the pacyente may not g^ to the
drawght nor pysse as he would / it is deedly (as Albuca. saith) therefore ye
shall not take that in cure. If there be no such accydente than take it in cure
take away the impostumacyon and payne. Ye shall salve the hole back without the
fracture. And ye shall let them blood shortly on the hand ne'er the thombe /
afterward set with your hand the bones and knots of the backebone as well as
ye can / and on the frature lay this plaster following.

A Plaster.

going to the draught. If there be a wound wide with with a great fracture it
is deedly nevertheless s^we it. And if there be any bones de{per}ted / de{per}te
them hole. And lay on the seem this powder following.

Of the brekyng of the shoulder. Ca. lxiiij.
IF the shoulder be broken ye shall set your seruaunt to hold the pacyent /
tha shall you with your one hand take the fracture / with the other hand
hold the shoulder / bone / and draw ye bones to gyder that they may come even
/ and lay upon the fracture the plaster the which I have learned you before.
And about the place lay the defensiuu / over the plaster of. iiij. fold lynnen
clothe / lay over that a little fine tow and than splentes made of leather of
willow wood in the gretnes after the shoulder bone / and over the splentes tow
dept / and bind them well / and ordre them in going to the drawght / dress
them at every. iij. days ones / as ye bones be well fastens salve them with
dyalthea / or with a falue that I shall hereafter write in the chaptre of the
brekynge of the rybbes. And ye time of fastenynge of that shoulder bone is. xx.
days (as Albucasis saith)

Of the brekyng of the bone above the elbow. Ca. lxv.
THe hygheste pipe of the arm is called Adiutoriu / it is sometime broke
ouertwhart / sometime a length / and (...) me nyghe by the joint / and that is
evil to heal. Therefore I will give you the learning and manner how ye shall
every fracture and brekynge handell and help. The first ye shall have. ij.
servants. ye one shall hold the pacyent above the arm next the shoulder.
and the other by the harde and on the elbow / and bid him draw it out by
little and little / and hold the patient so fast that he styre not his arms.
After that take with both your hands ye upper most pipe nyghe by the elbow
an (...) it softly on all sides till that ye here the bone craks. and that ye
se the howynge of the arm even. And when ye know the fracture / first ye
shall take the powder in the. (...)* chaptre. yt is made of walstone / wall^ /
or ambra in latin* c and the white of eggs / bades / splentes / pypes / nedles
/ oil of roses / and all that is needful. And when you have all your ger ready
/ and if the upper part of the arm be broken / set him in a chair. And if it
he in the leg / lay ye pacyent on a plain table / and one of your seruautes
to hold him over his belly / ye other servant to hold by the shoulder / and
the third to hold by the arm under ye elbow and bid him hold fast till ye
command him to pull. Then take that powder aforesaid in the. lix. chaptre, so
much till ye have enough / and mydle it with white of eggs and do thereto alume
as great as a wallnotte / and styre it well to gyder like pap Then take. ji.
fold of lynnen clothe and let it be so broad that it may come above under ye
fracture a quarter broad that is together half a yard / and after that the
pacyet be great or little / and that same cloth shall be a yard lunge in ye
manner as I have written of the brodnes. The clothe must be well stricken on the
one side with the salve / and laid on a bank. And ye must have a roll bound
of. ii. fadume lunge / a finger broad / and that must be rolled up / be stryke
a yard lunge with the forenamed things / lay it by the other salved band. And
after that ye shall take good splentes thynne as I have before write they must
be boude with small cords above / in the midst / also benethe / and as ye will
lace a dowbled of some mannys / so shall ye lace the ^len^es that the cords
may come without on the splentes / and let the splentes lye also next the
cloth / ye shall have also. iij. strong pypes of goose legs / or of elder
pypes / and ^pype boude with a strong small corde an^ stycke goth troughe the
pypes / and as all this is ready / than command ye other servant that holds
the arm by the elbow or leg hand or foot / that he properly styfly ^l /
and in the pulling set the bone even again and upon the other / and take heed
that ye knee / or elbow / hade / or foot stand up right / and you with your
flat hade over the fracture stryke so that ye about nor under feel none uneven
place / then shall ye loke if ye arm or leg be a straw breed longer then the
hole arm or leg is / if not it is right / I have scene a good master yt /
bound a pacyent thinking that ye broken leg was longer than the hole leg
/ he bound him so / when he had laid him in his bed the leg was weary short
/ then he asked me how that might be / I said / you have not laid ye pacyet
even hye enough with his hyppes which hath dysceyued you / therefore take he de
when ye bind them lay them lunge on the board and stretch them out / and when
he is so laid / then shall you se if the shynnes be crooked or not / and when you
have all things well marked / and all your ger is ready by you / so bydyoure
servants that they at all times hold fast / beware of shaking as it is
possible / then after that do about the bone the strykynge cloth / and lay it
even without wrymplyng after that take the roll band / and the last the small
cords with the pypes / as soon as that is done / than turn ye vppermoost
pipe and threst that stycke thorough / then turn the other pipe / after ye
third pipe ye shall know that the myddell pipe over ye fracture must
stronglyste be turned / for to^ ye ye vtermoost pipe harde / and the
myddelmoost not harde for so should ye blood run under the byndynge and make
the soon ^an impostumacyo swellynge / therefore ye myddelmoost pipe shall first
be bound. Will ye know if it be right bound about the fracture or not /
ask the pacyent if he have any payne in the bone or styckynge in the fracture
/ If he say than that he hath none / thinks he should thereon well go / and
that ye^ the broken leg longer then the hole leg / that is a sure token
that the byndynge is very good / and if ye shynne of ye leg be crooked like
abowe and even when ye have stricken over with your hand and so fullfyll the
crokednes with the pluma cyoles or with a fylte that ye splentes may lye even
on / and ye arms or legs shall (...) a (...) pipe so as I have learned in the
(...) chapytre / and take heed that the arm (...) that he may not loke in his
hand / ye leg that he may se the innerparte of the (...)* Take heed therefore
in the byndynge / for I have scene thereof much harm But the old masters as
(...) (...) (...) francus saith. If any arm be broken that shall ye know
thus. Lay your one hand on the fracture your other hand under ye fracture /
your seruaunt shall hold the pacyent by the arm and by the elbow / tha shall
ye the arm properly meue as I have said before. And than yt may know why there
it be broken or not / and if it be broken lay thereon shortly this plaster
following.

A plaster.
Take Mylstuffe meele / or Cycer meele / or Been meele or Barley meele. xij.
diuices. Masticis / Draganti Go^ arabici / of each. i^. ounc. (...) / (...) of
each one ounce. i^ is ^dled together with the white (...) / another of made a
plaster / stryke it upon a lynnen clothe a^ have learned before and that number
shall be stretched bound with splentes / with cords / with pypes in all manner
as I have showed you. (...) take heed that ye bind not the membre to fast for
thereof comes great harm / and the membre lost. And with out taking good heed
the pacyent is like to lose his ly^. Also it is good to stryke the defensiuum
to defend the membre from all ill accydent that thereto might come.

A def^nsiuum
This defensiuum shall be made of Oil of roses / Boloarme no / Uynegre /
nightshed sap or liquor solatri in latin / or sap of hou^eke / And let the
pacyeent s^tely on the vain blood, and that he hold his go^ynge to the
drawght. and he must also been his arm in a towel about his neck. and he
may be healed in. xiiij. days. And if there be any broken bone in the wounds ye
must take heed thereto. Or if there be ^bones departed into that shall you so one
take out as I have learned you in the chaptre of ye wound in the arms. And
some Surgean weans that the pacyent should dye when ye mary comethout of the
bone / that is not so A good cause why / for as ye flesh grows so grows
the mary. And after that ye sow the wound / and (...) thereto every day as In
the. xiiij. chaptre have said. And you shall or deyne them it that ye splentes
couere not the wound / and upon this wound ye shall straw every day this
powder.

A Powder.
Take Mastyke / (...) ye of araby of each. ij. ounces / Mumie / dragons blood
(...) an ounce / This powder hath (...) power to heal and to make strange the

hou^ ounces and of ye foresaid powder in ounces / (...) together / and thereof

Of the brekyng of the arm byneth the elbow. Ca. lxvj.
IN the nether (...) of the arm is. ij. pypes (...) it happens ye one is
broken / and sometime to the / and sometime it is bowed in the flesh without
brokynge (...) pla^ saith) ye fracture of the vnderpype is worse then yt (...)
pipe (As auicenna saith / and Alb^ also) if they be both broke it is ye worse
yt which ye may know by the feeling. if ye bones be broken then it cracked /
is it only bowed then it cracked not. And if both ye pypes be broken / so
must you have; ij. serpents yt one shall hold the pacyet by ye elbow and the
other by the hand and let the forehande strange downward to the groude / and
as it shall be drestyd it shall not living town. but do as I have showed before.
Or ye begin ye must have all your ger ready that is needful as I have
aforesaid. And as all is ready / take the pacyent before you / and command
your (...) to (...) him as is aforesaid / and set the bone with your hand
softly together in ye best manner that ye can / and do not the patient to much
payne in the setting in of the bones / because ye through such payne al the
moystnes dy^edeth in to the arm / and so might thereof come much harm /
whereby he should be in peril of death And when ye have set the arm together
after your best manner / take a loge broad bade depte in oil of roses and
threst out again and bind ye arm therewith / and defend ye arm from
moystnes and impostumacyon / and take away the great payne. and after yt dress
him as I have aforesaid. The yonge parsone that can not take heed to him self
dress him with that powder and the whyt of eggs as I have aforesaid If the
one pipe is hole and the other is broke / work therein as they were both broke
so as I have aforesaid / then alone ye need not to have so many splentes. And
when ye have them well dressed let them take heed of blood lettynge / and to the
draught going. And after yt the arm shall be salved with the defenssuu that
ye be sure of the sw^rynge and of ye impostumacyon / and bring the arm in a
towel about his neck and make a board lay thereon a co^yn taht the arm may
thee on rest. If there be a wound with ye fracture ^ake heed that ye bone fit
again And if there be any bones departed take it soon out / and then (...)
your spletes thereafter / and bind it well / so that ye may se ye woude / and if
it be need sow ye wound / and let the one end be open wherthrough it may be
cleansed. And lay on the seem ye foresaid powder / and the foresaid
mundificatiuum plaster that I in the chaptre before have learned / and do
therabout the defensiuu / and heal it as you heal another wound / as I have
learned in the next chaptre. And so let rule them of mete and drink / going
to the draught / as is aforesaid.

Of the brekyng of the fyngers / or Joyntes. Ca. lxvij.
IN the hand there is. iiij. bones. In every finger iij. bones. for their
final nes and strength they a seldom broken. And if it happen yt they break /
take heed that ye set them together after the best manner yt you can. Albucasis
saith that ye shall stretch the heed upon the table / and threst it in again /
and ye shall lay thereupon the plaster aforesaid in ye. lxv. chaptre. and in
this brekinge is no need to lay thereon clothes with oil of roses / but lonely
the plasters or the powder of walstone / as I have said in the. lix. chapytre
mydled with the white of an egg / lay the plaster on both sides of ye hand /
and thervpo lay splentes and tow after the length and breed of the hand /
afterward bind ye hade well after ye best manner / on ye hade tow / or an eve
board yt the. iiij syngers be together / and ye palm of the hand / and the
fyngers shall be filled with tow or with some other thing / bind ye hand so
(...) splentes may come to the half arm without and within / and when that is
done salve ye arm about with ye elbow with the defenciuum as is aforesaid /
then hang the arm in a towel or a cloth about his neck / if the fyngers be
broken / make ye splentes so lunge as the fyngers and more yt it may come in ye
midst of the hand / and bind with ye plaster as I have learned you in the
chapytre of the vppermoost pipe of ye arm / these bones shall be dressed every.
iiij. day ones / and at every time shall y^ salut it with desenciuum / and let
him hold the rule of going to the draught / And if ye fracture be with a
wound / then dress the wound every day / lay there upon a cloth depte in
oil of roses and lay therein ye powder of mumie / dragos blod like as I have
learned in ye. lxv. chapytre / and lay therouer the same plaster in the chapytre
aforesaid / and I was wont to take elder pypes / and lay the finger therein /
and it is very good yt ye bind the hole finger with ye woude fyngers / and so
gets the wounded fyngers quyckenes with the hole finger.

Of the broke bones in the breast. Ca. lxviij
THe breast is set together of. vij. bones and they be fastened with vij. rybbes /
therefore take heed in what place the bone is broken / lay your hand thereon feel
if it be broken / if it crake it is broke / and if not be broke but so (...)
bowed in / the^ take heed if he perbrake blood / for and if he perbrake blood /
it (...) evil token that there is some veins broken And if not but is otherwise
broken / th^ take your hand and threst upon the (...) place that the bone come
in again. Or (...) upon the fracture a great (...) or bore^ out pryckyng (...)
pulls out the bone again. And that so done sayther^ this plaster / which
has the strength to ca^ wed bones to come out again.

A Plaster.
Take Cy^ meal / or been meel. xij. ounces Draganti / (...) arabici / of (...)
ounces. Bolo arm^. iiij. ouces. make these together in (...) powder mydled with
the white of eggs / and thereof make a plaster and laid thereon / and dress
them it every iij. days ones. And as ye have boude them first let them be let
blood out of the (...) of the liver on the hand between the small finger and
the golden finger, and salve th^ place with defensiuu therein is no vynegre /
for the vynegre is cold / and the bones therein is subtle / and let the vynegre
(...) and hold the pacyent going to the drawghte, and make the place strong
in this salve.

A Salve.
Take Masticke / ^ankensence / of each (...) ounce / (...) BOlus (...) / of
each. (...) ounces. (...). (...) ounces / wa^e ij. ounces. (...) iiij. ounces.
Oil of olyue. xij. ouces, (...) the ware and roiyll with the oil by the fire /
and than strain it and ye other substance made in powder (...) therein / and
meddle them well together / and salve the place every day (...). And rule him with
mete and drink / as I have learned before.

Of the brekyng of the rybbes. Ca. lxix.
E shall know that there is xij. rybbes. vij. lunge. v. short / and the short
rybbes break not nyghe by the back / ye other. vij. rybbes break in many
places / and they be sotime crooked bowed inward and is not broke / sometime
outward also / and sometime ye fracture is deedly by loge endurynge of payne /
and sotyme it is not deedly and shortly healed / this ye may know hereafter
written / the first ye must with your hade feel the broke place / and if there
be any crackynge then is it broke / if there benone evil accidence to se it is
good to help / and as they bow inward so be they evil to help / And if the
rybbes be far sonken in / that the mebres be sore wounded inward it is
deedly / or el les lunge sykenes / and that may be know by the short brethe /
and by blood spyttynge coghynge with the stytche and payne in the side / thereof
often comes great payne to the rybbes / and to help the foresaid rybbes is
evil to do bring to pass / comely as they break the will depart or be
bowynge the one part inward / and the other outward / that inward is evil to
bring again to his place / and some parsones saith yt the pacyent should ete
such meets which should cause him to swell to fill him full of wind by yt
which ye rybbe should be drive again in to his right place / some saith to set
on them ventoses or boxes whereby ye rybbe should come outward / but it is to
doubt yt by that workynge of the bentoses or bores should come more harm.
Therefore dy^ death the old doctors in this cure. And ^ly abbas / Albuca.
Auicena / and (...) other have given many a cure to thyse. Auice. Albucasys /
they take thereto only / wol^ / oil powder / and bands. Rogerius brengeth it
in again to his place with cleuynge plasters cha^ed against the fire / or in
bathes and after that he makes ye rybbe fast. And Brunus makes that it comes
outward eve / and he lays thereon a plaster with splentes as thereto belongs /
and that part that is bowed inward he makes moist. Auice. occupied woll / oil
/ to a small inbowyng but and if it be great he pulls it outward Milhelmus de
Salice to makes eve with his hands / and with the white of an egg and meele
/ and with things laid thereon plaster wise. Lancfrancus did as Roger but he
commanded that the pacyent shall be helped with koughynge and nesynge to threft
the rybbe out. Guydo doth as Brunus / as the fracture comes outward / and
after yt he lays a plaster thereupon with the whyt of an egg with meele and
other conglutinatiuum / with flocks / or with tow / and splent^ it with a
piece of harde leather / binds the place ^ast with a lunge band / and at ye
last he softens the fracture with ^yalthea / or with oxicrato. But if the
fracture gothe inward / do as Rogerius and Lafranc{is} did. and if it be
needful tha he opens the fracture / or set thereupon a ventose or a box.
Thereafter he laid upon yt side on the fourth or fyfte day the white of an
egg / and oil of roses with tow for to drive away the payne and ipostumacyon
/ tha he boude it with a light byndynge / but reasonably to hold the medicine.
Then may ye rule the fracture with a plaster of been meel and honey / and the
raster soft it with Dyalthea / or with Occ^rato. Or lay thereupon this plaster
when ye have made it again even.

A Plaster.
Take Cycer meele / or Mylstoffe meele / or Been meele. xii. ounces. Masticke
/ Dragantum / Gummi arabici / Mumie / Boli armeni / of each anouce. powdered and
mydled with the white of eggs / and so lay it thereon / and salve it
roundaboute with the desensinu / then bind it and let it lye so. iii. days.
and let him blood on the hand / and rule him in going to the draught. and
cause him to lye hye / or upon his belly. And when ye have fastens all the
rybbes / salve them with this salve in the stead of Dyalthea and occicrato.

A Salve.
Take Rosell. vi. ounces. Uaxe. ij. ouces. Bdellii / Apoponati / of each an
ounce. Mastick / Frankincense / of each. ii. ouces. Dragons blood. Mumie of
each an ouce. Oil olyue. xvi. ounces. weke the Bdellium and thereafter in the
oil also / and do therein the other things made in powder / and mydle it so
together / and therewith salve every day the place of the fracture. for this
salve drives away the payne / and takes away all hardness. yt more over in ye
beginning how the ventoses or boringe more draws / the more moystnes sends
the nature thereto.

Therefore ye must regard if the pacyent be full of flesh moystnes so let him
blood on the other side against the fracture on the vain Basilica. and
thereafter on the same side of the fracture. But if ye pacyet be len work
after the learning of Rogerius / Jamericus / and Lafranc{is} / and anoynte your
hand with Turpetyn / or with melted pytche or with other clyuynge things / and
it shall be done in a bath / or by a fire / and lay it on the part yt is bowed
inward / and ye other hand on the part that stands outward for to threste
it downward / and with pulling of the adiutorium or arm pipe of ye body /
with koughynge / or holding in the wind. and quickly pull your hand upward
as often as the koughe / and holding of wind comes / for it pulls out ye
rybbes. But if it may not be so / and that ye pacyent be leave / or yt ye be in
fere / than ye shall follow the workynge of Auicenna. with setting the
ventoses or boxes. and as the rybbes be brought again in to their right places
/ so shall you bind it with tow depte with ye white of an egg meddled with meele /
or with the plaster of Mumie as I have learned. And when ye have thus wrought ye
payne swag not away nor ye rybbe will not come out again / and ye cough and
payne / and the stytche increases / the blood lettynge is done / and the
accedece comes thereto / so must ye cut on the rybbe / and vncouere the flesh
fro ye rybbe / take it away with your instrument / and ye shall lift upward the
rybbe again / for to compelle the coming of the accidence / afterward ye
shall heal it like another wound / to hold the rule of one pleutericus till
the axes and evil accidence be done.

Of the brekyng of the hyppes. Ca. lxx.
THis bone is broken sometime by strokes / sometime by falling / and sometime
cloven / and sometime bowed inward This fracture is known by feeling with your
hand in the strykynge over / if ye find any vneue. If the bone cleue / lay
thervpo this plaster. Been meal dragantum / mastycke. c. as I have before said
/ lay thereon plumacioles of tow or of lynen cloth and with spletes / and bind
it with a band about the body / and than so we it as often as it goth about /
at every sowynge set on a splente / and so let it lye. v. days / but not so
lunge if it happen that the pacyent be come syke or any evil accidence come to
him / Je shall let him blood between the little synger the gold finger / and
hold the rule of going to the draught so yt he abide thyn in his belly / and
if ye know by the foresaid token that the bone is broke it is need that ye
bone be threst downward and set again upright with ye help of your seruautes /
and that so done / lay thereupon the foresaid plaster and plumacyoles / and the
splentes shall be so great yt the bone may be touered withal / Over the
splentes lay other splentes / and depte tow threst out again / and the lay
over yt your byndynge with your best manner / and salve with that defesiuum /
and let the byndynge lye till ye iii. or. iiii. day be past. Je shall let him
blood prycke on his back / rule him with mete drink as I have often said to you
before.

Of the brekyng of pypes about the kne. Ca. lxxj.
AS ye know by the feeling of ye bone whether it be broken with a woude / or
without a wound If it be broken without a wound / so is it needful to make
all your ger ready yt thereto belongs / as I have learned you in the. lix.
chapytre / then shall ye set the bone together again / as I have learned you in
ye. lxv. chapytre / in the brekynge of the arms and of ye legs / and as the
bone is well set together / then shall you deep ye clothe in oil of roses / and
wind it well about the leg or take the powder of walstone so as I have
learned so shull you take the oil the plaster made of been meal / as I have
often learned you / with the white of an egg stryke on a cloth and lay over ye
bades splentes small cords pypes / as I have plainly learned you in ye lxv.
chapytre of the brekynge of the arms about the elbow / your bands and
spletes shall be so ordained and to close the bone / hole it to gyder / and
take heed yt ye the bone bind not to styffe / whereby thereto may come no
swelling nor other harm for that is to be feared / for they be daedly tokens /
now as all things is well done / so shall ye ye pacyent command to lye down
/ and he shall not meue. Also ye old masters dyscordeth howe the pacyent
shall lye / Rogerius / Al bucasys / and wylhelmus / lay them in an eve place or
bed / wyndeth his leg softly with clothes and tow^ / and some other masters /
as master Peter / he hath. ii. lunge splentes to the end of ye foot that is
woude with lynne clothe and binds thervpo. iii. or. iiii. bands / Auicenna /
Brunus / and Rogerius / splenteth with. ii. lunge spletes bound to the foot /
But Lancfrancus and the yonge masters lay the leg along / all the masters
menynge is that the broke leg shall be laid eve to rest without ouy hurtynge
that it shall not be moved / and the pacyent shall not move on no side / and
also thereof spreketh Rogerius to ye work master that he shall keep straight
the membre and save his health / and also of a more surety Romanus lays ye
pacyent on a small bed / under the which bed is an hole thrugh the which he
may do his water / and easement without lyftynge of him self / and he must be
bound to. iii. or. iiii. places of ye bedstede and ye hole foot must be
bound to the stock that ye pacyent may not draw it up to him and Guy do
binds him with lunge splentes / and lays on him awayght binds to his
foot a plomet of lead / and as al this is ordained and done / so shall he
hold and rule as I have said in ye. lix. chapytre with blod lettynge / draught
going / and with eating and drinking / ye shall dress him diligently. iiii.
v. or. vi. days together / with out he be very syke / and at every dressynge / ye
shall loke well on the leg how it is dys^osed / and if it be not set upright
/ take head give him his right form / therefore take ye heed in ye first
begynynge / for if it should stoned lunge / it ware harde to make it up right /
is there one wound great / then se if there be any bone de{per}ted that take
away / and sow the wound / and on ye seem straw that powder that belonght
thereto / as in the xiii chaptre is said / and as ye the membre has set up
right well together / than take wode splentes after the degre of the woude that
the splentes towche not the woude / when ye bind the leg / and also that ye
may every day se to the wound / and on the woude lay the aforsayd powder yt be
laid upon the seem and upon that powder lay ye mundificatiuum plaster.

A Plaster.
Take rose honey streynyd. xii. ouces. Barley meele / Mylstuf meele / or other
like. xii. ounces / of this powder that lies on the some. take. ii. ounces.
and meddle this together and as the wound with this plaster is cleansed / than
heal it with the grene woundyd plaster / and with powder of Cypressenottes /
Mumie / Frankesence / Gall apple / of each ii. ounces. and on the seem lay the
powder of Dragons blood / Dragatum / Gomy of Arabice / of each like much / and
all this mydle together.

Of the brekyng of the knee shyue or whirl bone. Ca. lxxij.
When the knee shyue is broken / then set it again ^p right with your hand in
the best manner as it can be than lay thervp ye plaster made of Barley meele / or
Been meele / and Dragons blood c. (...) ye shall not lay there on the clothe
dept in rose oil / and as the plaster is laid thereon / than stryke there about
the Defensiuu / and thereafter lay upon it Plumacioles of tow / and thereafter
splentes / and the rover bind it well with ye band and sow the band with whyt
thread / and let him blood on ye hand / between the little finger and the
gold finger / rule him in going to draught / and with meet and drink / as is
a foresaid.

An instrument to make a crooked knee right.



Of the brekyng of the shynne. Ca. lxxiij.
IN the shynne be. ii. bones sometime they be broke both / sometime brekyth the
great pipe / sometime the little pipe. As the great pipe is broken / then is
ye fracture behind. If the small pipe is broken / tha the fracture is before
on the shynne / but the great is more peryllous tha the small. And as they
both be broken that is most peryl Then it is needly to have al your ger ready
by you that thereto belongs or serves.

Than must ye have. ii. servants / the one to hold the knee / and the other
the fete. command to pull in al manner / and to be ready as I have learned you in
the. ixv chaptre. of the brekynge of the arms. feeling with your hand if it
stand upright / and loke if the broken leg is a little longer than the hole
leg. In like wise loke if the hole leg is crooked or not. And bind it than
will, a clothe depte in oil of roses / or with ye powder of waltstone / with
ye baundes and spletes. And the splentes must be longer in the brekynge of the
leg than in the brekynge of the arm. and with the small cords / pypes. Je
shall ask the pacyent as it stands in the same chaptre / yt ye may know if
it be right bound or not. And take he de that ye bind him not to styfe /
whereby the pacyent might come in great harm / payne and fere. And should him
bring in paralisys / and the pacyent might also by the harde byndyng lyse that
membre. And defend the place with the defensyuum. and beware you of the harde
byndynge. And let him blood on the hand / and hold him in ye rule in his
drawght going. And ye shall dress him in. iij. or. iiii. days onys. If that
leg be broken with a wound / than loke if there any bones be beparted / they
shall be taken out. And fere you not if ye mary goth therout. Sow the wound /
than make vpryggt the bone again / set it to gyder and make splentes / but
do so that ye may se to the woude at every day. To these brekynge of the
leg be sondry manners of spletes. Je shall make a hole form after ye gretenes
and length of the leg / there as the iegge shall be laid in with ye byndynge
This woude is sore deedly / but fere not so much the fracture as the wound.
It is needful to defend the wound from the impostumcyo with the defensyuum /
and mundificatiuu / that no great moystnes nor swerynge come thereto / for the
pacyent might thereof dye.

Therefore it is nedely to loke thereto at every day onys / and tha if irnedyth
the bone shall be ryghtyd without payne. And upon the seem straw the powder of
the sowynge Than heal the wound / like the woude with the brekynge of the arm /
or in the shoulder rule the pacyent in etynge and drinking as I have often
aforesaid.

Of the bone brekynge in the calfe of the leg. Ca. lxxiiij
SEldom is broken the bone of the calfe / for it is an harde bone / and is
defendyd with the strings synewes (as Guy do saith) but Wylhelm{is} said If it
happens that the bone of the calfe be broken / that shall be kowen with
feeling. And that bone breketh sometime with strokes / sometime with falling /
than help him thus.

A Plaster.
Take been meel / or Cycermeel / or mylstuf meel. xii. ounces. mastick /
Dragantu / Gomi arabici / of each. ij. ounces. Bolus arm nus. i. ounce. all
this made in small powder than mydle it with the whyt of eggs / and lay it on
the fracture / and therouer the spletes with tow. and therouer the band / and
sow it with a thread at each wyndynge of the band. And the band shall go roude
about the hole foot / round about the half leg and salve the foot with the
defensiuum / and rule him as is aforesaid.

Of the brekyng of the bone abowe upon the foot. Ca. lxxv.
AS raseta that is the bone of ye foot is broke it is harde to heal like
Auicena wrythet / and thereto comet oftetymes i'll accedes. ye breking (as
albucasis said) shall be helped thus / set the foot of the pacyent on the
groude / and lay ye bone right as it best may be done / tha splente him with a
broad splent after ye sole of the foot / and lay on the fracture the foresaid
plaster made of that powder meddled with the white of an egg / over the plaster
lay splentes with tow / dept in the white of an egg / than shall ye set the
foot on a soole of wood / and about the foot stryke that defesiuum / than
bind the foot pull therouer a sho of fy^t / let him blood / Is the woude with
a fracture tha bind him every day onys or dress him / and lay on the wound
this plaster.

A plaster.
Take Mastyck / Dragantum / Gommy of Arabyci of each. ii. ounces / Mumye /
Bolus Armenus / of each one ouce / This powder meddled with rose honey / make
thereof a plaster / there with the wound shall be cleansed / there after heal the
